## Thread: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl) — 18 msgs

### Re: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl)

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.c
- **Message-ID:** `<6s2715$dng$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<35e08cf0.3273969@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <3298232.76073.22283@kcbbs.gen.nz> <35df95c8.636306@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <I1%D1.448$Kt4.2131068@news1.atlantic.net> <6rqiq9$ehg@lotho.delphi.com> <ZYdE1.473$Kt4.2409046@news1.atlantic.net> <6s007l$4u2@lotho..delphi.com> <904129648snz@genesis.demon.co.uk> <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com> <6s1fsg$2io@news-central.tiac.net>`

```
>I find this fascinating, because I was on the team that built the first
>COBOL compiler for the PDP-10 and therefore paid a lot of attention to
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>their COBOL implementations.


By golly.  My milk machine.  The first Cobol I coded was on your
compiler<g>, about 68-69.  That was also the machine I learnt
my first Fortran and my first assembler on.  The first major Fortran
program that I ever worked on was a bootstrap Pascal compiler
to port native Pascal (in Pascal) to the 10.

Modern compilers could learn a lot from Tops-10, in my opinion
one of the most innovative and best OS's ever written.  As I recall,
I could say "COMPILE ABC" where ABC.CBL was a Cobol program,
that called a Fortran program, that called an assembler program, and
the compile command was smart enough to figure out which language
to call for each module, compile all three using the appropriate language,
then link them ... all done completely invisibly and from the single
command.  Nice job.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl)

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.c
- **Message-ID:** `<6s2uc1$c5e$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<35e08cf0.3273969@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <3298232.76073.22283@kcbbs.gen.nz> <35df95c8.636306@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <I1%D1.448$Kt4.2131068@news1.atlantic.net> <6rqiq9$ehg@lotho.delphi.com> <ZYdE1.473$Kt4.2409046@news1.atlantic.net> <6s007l$4u2@lotho..delphi.com> <904129648snz@genesis.demon.co.uk> <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com> <6s1fsg$2io@news-central.tiac.net> <6s2715$dng$1@news.igs.net> <35E4D974.570@inficad.com>`

```

>> I could say "COMPILE ABC" where ABC.CBL was a Cobol program,
>> that called a Fortran program, that called an assembler program, and
>> the compile command was smart enough to figure out which language
>> to call for each module, compile all three using the appropriate
language,
>> then link them ... all done completely invisibly and from the single
>> command.  Nice job.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Damned if I know.  Maybe because every tool is designed by a different
vendor?  Maybe because nobody likes command line interfaces anymore?
Even a good make command seems hard to come by these days. Not
one of the "integrated environments" that I have seen in the last five years
has the capability of supporting a second language.  In fact, most of them
bury the command structure so deeply that it is almost impossible to write
one for lack of information.  The pseudo-make that I am using at the
moment has wonderful pictures compared to ten years back, but cannot
handle a project with more than one main program more than one level
of dependency, nor even a filename that does not conform to it's own
private notions of file-naming conventions. There is no way to maintain
a library, or update copy books, etc. etc. etc.  How anybody that has ever
written a program greater than 1000 lines long could design a make
that is non-iterative I will never understand.  Even a DOS batch can call
DOS batch.<grumble mode on>
;<)

Maybe I should write one in Cobol.  The damned thing is just a tree
structure of dependencies.
```

```

Thane Hubbell wrote in message ...
>On Fri, 28 Aug 1998 05:32:51, paulr@bix.com (paulr) wrote:
>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>

<grin> You can stop wondering, yes it does. On workstations of course,
which is where you would need native support. Of course, if you mean NATIVE
as in, can it support mainframe (i.e. OS/390 Big Endian format) then you are
still in luck, because, yes it can. With a simple flip of a compiler switch.

The basic file formats supported are:

STL - provides the basic facilities for local files, and supports
sequential, relative, and
indexed files.

VSAM- wihch allows reading and writing files on remote systems with COBOL
I/O statements,
and also supprts sequential, relative, and indexed file types. Under OS/2 or
CICS 4.1 on NT,
you can also have local VSAM files.

BTRIEVE- files are also supported. If you use the BTRIEVE file system, this
also gives
you access to CICS files under OS/2.

STL files have the following characteristics:
    Minimum record size: 1 byte
    Maximum record size: 65536 bytes
    Max record key length: 255 bytes
    Max number or alternate keys: 253 bytes
    Max relative key value: 2**32 -1
    Max number of bytes allocated for a file: 2**32 -1

VSAM files have the following characteristics: (since they can be local...
:)
    Minimum record size: 1 byte
    Maximum record size: 64,000 bytes
    Max record key length: 255 bytes
    Max relative key value: 2**32 -2
    Max number of bytes allocated for a file: 2**32

>What is the method of GUI creation?  API calls?  Other method?
>

It is an API of course (what isn't?) but it is actually done with a very
cool front end.
The paradigm expressed is an almost total separation of presentation logic
from
the business logic- visual parts vs. non-visual parts. The actual code
produced uses
a fairly standard call using interface to do the work, but I don't think I
have ever
had to modify a single compiler generated screen yet, that is, modify it by
hand.

I think they have a free download on the ibm site, but I would attempt to
get the docs with
it first. It is VERY upwards compatible, to the point you can actually do
remote edit/compile/
debug cycles with it. It also has very good support for CICS and DB2 SQL.
(Naturally! :)

-Paul
```

Donald Tees (donald@willmack.com) wrote:

: >> I could say "COMPILE ABC" where ABC.CBL was a Cobol program,
: >> that called a Fortran program, that called an assembler program, and
: >> the compile command was smart enough to figure out which language
: >> to call for each module, compile all three using the appropriate
: language,
: >> then link them ... all done completely invisibly and from the single
: >> command.  Nice job.
: >
: >
: >Why can't we have that now?
: >
: Damned if I know.  Maybe because every tool is designed by a different
: vendor?  Maybe because nobody likes command line interfaces anymore?
: Even a good make command seems hard to come by these days. Not
: one of the "integrated environments" that I have seen in the last five years
: has the capability of supporting a second language.  In fact, most of them
: bury the command structure so deeply that it is almost impossible to write
: one for lack of information.  The pseudo-make that I am using at the
: moment has wonderful pictures compared to ten years back, but cannot
: handle a project with more than one main program more than one level
: of dependency, nor even a filename that does not conform to it's own
: private notions of file-naming conventions. There is no way to maintain
: a library, or update copy books, etc. etc. etc.  How anybody that has ever
: written a program greater than 1000 lines long could design a make
: that is non-iterative I will never understand.  Even a DOS batch can call
: DOS batch.<grumble mode on>
: ;<)

IBM got around this fairly successfully with their Workframe product,
used with VAC++ 3.0 for OS/2.  However, OS/2 died in the marketplace
and IBM are moving to a more 'normal', editor-based IDE.  A lot of
Workframe's functionality was based on IBM's Workplace Shell, a very
powerful GUI which it wouldn't be practical to port.  Still, it was
the best GUI/environment I've ever used.


Will
cwr@crash.cts.com
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl)

- **From:** John Porter <jdporter@min.net>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.c
- **Message-ID:** `<35E55C72.1E3D@min.net>`
- **References:** `<35e08cf0.3273969@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <3298232.76073.22283@kcbbs.gen.nz> <35df95c8.636306@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <I1%D1.448$Kt4.2131068@news1.atlantic.net> <6rqiq9$ehg@lotho.delphi.com> <ZYdE1.473$Kt4.2409046@news1.atlantic.net> <6s007l$4u2@lotho..delphi.com> <904129648snz@genesis.demon.co.uk> <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com> <6s1fsg$2io@news-central.tiac.net> <6s2715$dng$1@news.igs.net> <35E51718.621C0CA2@elmont.fi>`

```
Ari Lukumies wrote:
> 
> Would that be a little like 'cc' (compiler control) in Unix? (No, it
> doesn't stand for 'c compiler'.)

I think so.  In typical unixes (or at least SunOS, where my experience
is), all compilers (c, fortran, pascal, and whatever else) generate
object (code|files) which look just like the object generated by
the assembler; so once the objects are created by each compiler, the
objects can all be linked together, as if they were generated by the
assembler.  cc can dispatch the appropriate compiler (or the assembler),
based on the file name.  (Of course, to actually link such objects
together successfully, they have to cooperate on calling conventions,
which can take some work.)
```

<a id="t-4066bba261"></a>
## Thread: IBM mainframe COBOL - how to close an output file and reopen it with a different DSN — 5 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: IBM mainframe COBOL - how to close an output file and reopen it with a different DSN

- **From:** Ibis redibis numquam peribis <rrg10300@dasb.fhda.edu>
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Pine.OSF.3.95q.980826175342.20334j-100000@octane.dasb.fhda.edu>`
- **References:** `<6s0p5r$4v0$1@holly.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`

```


On Wed, 26 Aug 1998, Jim Gallagher wrote:

=> Hello,
=> I am new to the IBM mainframe environment (not new to COBOL) -I am writing
=> batch programs using COBOL II and DB2 in an OS390 environment. One program
=> must create a file for each of multiple institutions. The files would be
=> named BK.PROD.001INST, BK.PROD.002INST etc. Institution number is a 3
=> position field.  Is there a way in IBM COBOL (other than allocating and
=> declaring FDs for 999 files in my program) to handle this ? i.e. create
=> BK.PROD.001INST, when institution # changes close it and reopen it as
=> BK.PROD.002INST.
=> 
=> Any pointers would be most appreciated.  BTW this is not homework !!
> 
> Jim Gallagher
> 
Since I work in a VAX environment, this may be less than helpful, but VMS
allows us to keep multiple versions of a file within a directory. If we
open and close a file ten times, we end up with 
                  filename;10
                  filename;9
                  filename;8
                  filename;7
                  filename;6
                  filename;5
                  filename;4
                  filename;3
                  filename;2
                  filename;1
Then the DCL command procedure that invoked the COBOL program can run a
loop that renames the files appropriately:
      rename filename;1   BK.PROD.001INST
         .
         .
         .
      rename filename;10   BK.PROD.010INST

The DCL command proc stops renaming when it detects that filename;nnn does
not exist.

If the actual institution number can fall arbitrarily within the range
000-999, then you can write the actual sequence to a simple text file
and have the command procedure rename the filename;x according to the
values the command proc reads from the text file.

Before invoking the COBOL program, the command proc makes sure that there
are no files with the name filename;x either by deleting them or by
renaming the old versions to filename.backup.timestamp, or some such
thing. Ditto for the auxialry text file if you need to go that route.


rrg
===============================================================================
Hey, listen! I read it in the Encyclopaedia Britannica.
That is my story and I am sticking with it.
===============================================================================
```

#### ↳ Re: IBM mainframe COBOL - how to close an output file and reopen it with a different DSN

- **From:** jaw@mail.indigo.ie (John Whistler)
- **Date:** 1998-08-29T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<B20CF85796683B715@0.0.0.0>`
- **References:** `<6s0p5r$4v0$1@holly.prod.itd.earthlink.net> <Pine.OSF.3.95q.980826175342.20334j-100000@octane.dasb.fhda.edu>`

```

If you're using VAX COBOL, you can have the file name in working storage
using
the "value of id" clause. The name can be set by the program before opening
the
file. I wouldn't rely on having multiple versions of the file - there's too
much
danger of someone doing a purge at the wrong moment!

John Whistler.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: IBM mainframe COBOL - how to close an output file and reopen it with a different DSN

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol

```


On Sat, 29 Aug 1998, John Whistler wrote:
[excised]
=> the
=> file. I wouldn't rely on having multiple versions of the file - there's
=> too much danger of someone doing a purge at the wrong moment!
=> 
=> John Whistler.
> > > > > 
No danger at all iff'n you remove the delete permission on the file. Then
that file and all subsequent versions of it will rebuff the dreaded urge
to purge. Do it all the time. 

rrg

and see the copacetic side of things.

## Thread: Converting integers on a Unix system to a comp-3 format — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax) · [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### Converting integers on a Unix system to a comp-3 format

- **From:** mdavis9@aol.com (MDavis9)
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998082700391500.UAA22334@ladder01.news.aol.com>`

```
I need to convert a file with ASCII Integers to a comp-3 format, and
vice-versa.
Can anyone provide help with this ?
Thanks in advance.
Mike
```

#### ↳ Re: Converting integers on a Unix system to a comp-3 format

- **From:** "Leif Svalgaard" <leif@ibm.net>
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35e4aeae.0@news1.ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<1998082700391500.UAA22334@ladder01.news.aol.com>`

```

MDavis9 wrote in message
<1998082700391500.UAA22334@ladder01.news.aol.com>...
>I need to convert a file with ASCII Integers to a comp-3 format, and
>vice-versa.
>Can anyone provide help with this ?


when you ask for something like this be a bit more specific.
From the question as it stands you could do:

01  File1-record.
      02  File1-int1             pic 9(7).
      02  File1-int2             pic s9(3).

01  File2-record.
      02  File2-int1             pic 9(7) comp-3.
      02  File2-int2             pic s9(3) comp-3.

.....

read File1
move File1-int1 to File2-int1
move File1-int2 to File2-int2
write File2
loop until done

but there is probably more to it than that, so please explain.
```

### Re: 'Cutesie-Poo' Programmer's Tricks

- **From:** kiyoinc@ibm.XOUT.net (cory hamasaki)
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,comp.lang.cobol,bit.listserv.ibm-main
- **Message-ID:** `<7kepWhCNP4qd-pn2-O4wH6yMrNEMB@localhost>`
- **References:** `<6p804d$scb$1@clarknet.clark.net> <6p8fbr$s9f$1@news.igs.net> <6p9lfc$rta$1@news.enterprise.net> <6pa239$a56$1@clarknet.clark.net> <6pai39$krq1@odie.mcleod.net> <35B90ED3.4654@erols.com> <6pdasj$130c1@odie.mcleod.net>`

```
On Wed, 26 Aug 1998 14:09:04, GeorgeValentine@usa.net wrote:
 
> I have some, possibly stupid suggestions, that you may not have though of
> or may have considered too much trouble. (If you are considering having
> one built, that probably puts these back in contention.)

These are not stupid suggestions... well, most aren't, one is.
 
> First, IBM used to make systems in a truck for the Army, and it may be that
> one or more of them still exists somewhere.  If you have good contacts in
> the military you may be able to locate one.  I would assume that DOD
> has an inventory list somewhere. (Similarly, GAO or somebody probably
> has an inventory for civilian agancies, one of which may have one.)

Great tip, we were looking for a contact at the DLA, the Defense 
Logistic Agency but the GAO might have records on other systems.
 
> Second, if the trickle-down theory works, there must be some S370s in
> remote countries that you might be able to get to.  Along those lines, I
> would try Russia first.  I would expect that you would have to do some
> off-net scrounging, maybe with the help of the State Department.

I don't have any contacts at State but this is a good suggestion.
 
> Third, you might call the "maintenance" vendors, particularly those that
> are global, and see if they know of a site, in-country or out.

We've checked with exporters and domestic hardware maintenance
companies... no luck.  This is early 1970's technology and vanished in 
the late 1980's.
 
> Fourth, if you are into _very tedious_ you might have the pack disassembled,
> have the surfaces developed, and have the bits read microscopically. (I
> am assuming that the surface on the 3330 is similar to the surface on a
> tape so that it can, in fact, be developed.)

This is the one stupid suggestion... and I'm not pinging on you. We 
wasted several hours debating magnaflux development.  It got heated for 
a while. So we're the dummies, not you. 

Here's the problem, the device contains 200+ megabytes.  The magnaflux
will reveal the individual magnetic flux changes including IBGs,
ECCs/Parity, start-stop framing (if 3330's have such a thing).

To read the data, you have to read the ones and zeros, keeping track of 
positions. I can imagine someone staring into a stereo microscope and 
calling out "One, zero, one, one, one, zero".  At a ten cents a byte,
a penny a bit, this is a twenty million dollar labor charge.

It would be cheaper to have an engineering house mount a modern head on 
a stick and servo.  Even if they charge fifty grand to build the rig,
it's much cheaper and more reliable.
 
> Fifth, since money seems to be a small matter, why not get your client to
> offer a $10 or $20,000 finders fee for someone locating a usable 3330, and
> publicize it on the web? Enough people will probably make enough calls
> to find one if it exists.

Well, cost is always a consideration.  I've put hundreds of hours into
this, pro bono...  when services are free, the client doesn't mind 
asking you to check on just one more issue.
 
I don't blame them for that; it's human nature not to appreciate that 
which is free.  

> Good luck.
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> http://www.dejanews.com/rg_mkgrp.xp   Create Your Own Free Member
Forum

cory hamasaki  11,817 hours

```

## Thread: VB 5.0 and Inter-language Data Type Conversions (COBOL) and Parameter Passing — 2 msgs

### VB 5.0 and Inter-language Data Type Conversions (COBOL) and Parameter Passing

- **From:** "Gene Webb" <gene.webb@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol,microsoft.public.scripting.vbscript,microsoft.public.vb.enterprise,microsoft.public.vb.general.discussion,microsoft.public.vb.syntax
- **Message-ID:** `<6s2iph$p68@enews4.newsguy.com>`

```
Sorry if this is a newbie question.

I have an existing COBOL application that has a well defined interface.  I can create standard Windows DLLs with no problem. I can even create ActiveX or OCX controls with it. I am trying to find a solution to a problem dealing with returning a COBOL Structure (which will only use VB data types such as longs, strings, etc).  The problem is that the structure contains (as part of it anyway) arrays of fixed length strings separated by other data types such as long.  

For example

Visual Basic Definition 

dim myArray as String * 10
Type myBuffer
        myArray1[16] as myArray
        myArray2[16] as myArray
        myVar1 as Long
        myArray3[16] as myArray
        myVar2 as long  
End Type

NOTE: The syntax of this may not be exactly right, but, what I want is a string with a fixed width of 10 characters that occurs 16 times for the myArrayx.


COBOL Definition

01 myBuffer.
   05 myArray1  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
   05 myArray2  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
   05 myVar1    pic s9(9) comp-5.
   05 myArray3  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
   05 myVar2    pic s9(9) comp-5.
    
Because of the way VB handles strings I lose everything after the first array myArray2.  I am thinking  of try to just return a string buffer that is the length of all of the data in myBuffer.  The question is how do I separate out the long data and keep it in that format.  That is take four bytes out of a string and say this is really a long.

I hope this is clear enough.  Let us say we had a buffer 10 bytes long.  The first 4 bytes were a string.  The next 4 bytes were actually a long in native format and the last 2 bytes were a string.  How would you part the long out of the middle of that string and treat it as a long. The hex value of that four bytes would look something like X'00000001' for the number 1.  

Any thought would be welcome.
```

#### ↳ Re: VB 5.0 and Inter-language Data Type Conversions (COBOL) and Parameter Passing

- **From:** "Alejandro Lapeyre" <ael@arredamenti.com.ar>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol,microsoft.public.scripting.vbscript,microsoft.public.vb.enterprise,microsoft.public.vb.general.discussion,microsoft.public.vb.syntax
- **Message-ID:** `<emouJ#X09GA.120@uppssnewspub05.moswest.msn.net>`
- **References:** `<6s2iph$p68@enews4.newsguy.com>`

```
There is a difference in the definition of Strings and Fixed lenght strings
Strings are stored as pointer to null terminated strings
Fixed lenght strings are stored in place.
So:
01 myBuffer.
   05 myArray1  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
   05 myArray2  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
   05 myVar1    pic s9(9) comp-5.
   05 myArray3  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
   05 myVar2    pic s9(9) comp-5.

TYPE MyBuffer 
    myArray1 as string * 160
    myArray2 as string * 160
    myVar1 as long
    myArray3 as string*160
    myVar2 as long
END TYPE

or

TYPE MyBuffer 
    myArray1(0 to 159) as byte
...


    Gene Webb escribió en mensaje <6s2iph$p68@enews4.newsguy.com>...
    Sorry if this is a newbie question.
     
    I have an existing COBOL application that has a well defined interface.  I can create standard Windows DLLs with no problem. I can even create ActiveX or OCX controls with it.     End Type
     
    NOTE: The syntax of this may not be exactly right, but, what I want is a string with a fixed width of 10 characters that occurs 16 times for the myArrayx.
     
     
    COBOL Definition

       05 myArray1  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
       05 myArray2  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
       05 myVar1    pic s9(9) comp-5.
       05 myArray3  pic x(10) occurs 16 times.
       05 myVar2    pic s9(9) comp-5.
        
    Because of the way VB handles strings I lose everything after the first array myArray2.  I am thinking  of try to just return a string buffer that is the length of all of the data in myBuffer.     I hope this is clear enough.  Let us say we had a buffer 10 bytes long.  The first 4 bytes were a string.  The next 4 bytes were actually a long in native format and the last 2 bytes were a string.  How would you part the long out of the middle of that string and treat it as a long. The hex value of that four bytes would look something like X'00000001' for the number 1.

## Thread: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl) — 3 msgs

### Re: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl)

- **From:** paulr@bix.com (paulr)
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.c
- **Message-ID:** `<6s240o$hf2@lotho.delphi.com>`
- **References:** `<35e08cf0.3273969@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <3298232.76073.22283@kcbbs.gen.nz> <35df95c8.636306@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <I1%D1.448$Kt4.2131068@news1.atlantic.net> <6rqiq9$ehg@lotho.delphi.com> <ZYdE1.473$Kt4.2409046@news1.atlantic.net> <6s007l$4u2@lotho..delphi.com> <904129648snz@genesis.demon.co.uk> <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com> <p9VE1.609$Kt4.3375173@news1.atlantic.net>`

```
You know, we are not out to save the world here... if you lighten up
a bit you might have a lot more fun. 

What I said, and what I meant is there *is* no C without 
extensions - think of clib. It has to be there. 

-Paul



Rick Smith (ricksmith@aiservices.com) wrote:

: paulr wrote in message <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com>...
: >
: >Lawrence,
: >  Don't take this as an argument, because it isn't, but separating C
: >from platform libraries is like separating COBOL from ISAM. They
: >simply have to go together.

: There is a vital and distinct difference in the separation. If you
: separate platform libraries from C you (may) get standard C. If
: you separate ISAM from COBOL, you no longer have standard
: COBOL.

: > C is *built* that way, to be easily
: >extensible by anyone- that is in fact, at the core of the
: >language design. (Just as, belive it or not, text processing is
: >one of the core design features of UNIX. At least, that is how
: >they sold it to AT&T management when they wanted $$ to develop it. :)
: >
: > Without a copy of the white book close at hand, I can't dispute
: >anything you said regarding pointers, but I remember writing
: >interrupt handlers in C without much problem. :) Heck, I've written
: >interrupt handlers in Ada without a lot of problems, but I don't
: >think I could write one in COBOL.
: >

: The potential problems of writing device drivers in COBOL were
: addressed elsewhere and are not relevant to whether standard
: C could be used to write device drivers. My conclusion WRT COBOL
: was "not without extensions."
: -------------------------------
: Rick Smith
: e-mail: < ricksmith@aiservices.com >
```

#### ↳ Re: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl)

- **From:** Ari Lukumies <ari.lukumies@elmont.fi>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.c
- **Message-ID:** `<35E515D3.BF6131E5@elmont.fi>`
- **References:** `<35e08cf0.3273969@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <3298232.76073.22283@kcbbs.gen.nz> <35df95c8.636306@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <I1%D1.448$Kt4.2131068@news1.atlantic.net> <6rqiq9$ehg@lotho.delphi.com> <ZYdE1.473$Kt4.2409046@news1.atlantic.net> <6s007l$4u2@lotho..delphi.com> <904129648snz@genesis.demon.co.uk> <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com> <p9VE1.609$Kt4.3375173@news1.atlantic.net> <6s240o$hf2@lotho.delphi.com>`

```
paulr wrote:
> 
> You know, we are not out to save the world here... if you lighten up
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> extensions - think of clib. It has to be there.

You're confusing runtime libraries with platform specific libraries
here. Having ANSI C with platform specifics is roughly equal to having
ANSI COBOL with EXEC SQL... support. Having the ANSI specified
*standard* libraries with ANSI C is roughly equal to having the
necessary file, etc. support that ANSI COBOL specifies.

What comes to clib, ANSI C doesn't standardize that the runtime
environment (say, fopen, for example) be in libraries. You could (but I
doubt you would) write an ANSI C implementation in a single source file
that could be compiled to a single executable. Well, ANSI C requires you
to have a specified set of header files, though, so those should be
written as separate files... :)

	AriL
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl)

- **From:** engebret@sg1.cr.usgs.gov (Chris Engebretson)
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.c
- **Message-ID:** `<EyCt9J.5p2@igsrsparc2.er.usgs.gov>`
- **References:** `<35e08cf0.3273969@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <3298232.76073.22283@kcbbs.gen.nz> <35df95c8.636306@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <I1%D1.448$Kt4.2131068@news1.atlantic.net> <6rqiq9$ehg@lotho.delphi.com> <ZYdE1.473$Kt4.2409046@news1.atlantic.net> <6s007l$4u2@lotho..delphi.com> <904129648snz@genesis.demon.co.uk> <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com> <p9VE1.609$Kt4.3375173@news1.atlantic.net> <6s240o$hf2@lotho.delphi.com> <35E515D3.BF6131E5@elmont.fi>`

```
In article <35E515D3.BF6131E5@elmont.fi>,
        Ari Lukumies <ari.lukumies@elmont.fi> writes:

|> Well, ANSI C requires you to have a specified set of header 
|> files, though, so those should be written as separate files... :)

Actually, it makes no such requirement.  There is nothing in the
language standard that requires that <stdio.h> (for example) be an
actual, physical file that you can go out and look at with a text
editor.  The only behavior that it requires is that

    #include <stdio.h>

must introduce all of the declarations/macro definitions/etc. that
the standard requires it to.  This being the case, when a C
implementation encounters "#include <stdio.h>", it could simply go
out into its internal symbol tables and insert the appropriate
entries there without actually going out and reading a file.

There are C implementations that actually do this.

Regards,
```

## Thread: TSO ISPF, CLISTs and/or REXX exec — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Re: TSO ISPF, CLISTs and/or REXX exec

- **From:** gchomuk@my-dejanews.com
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6s1q6q$1t4$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`
- **References:** `<35DB9C35.4B24@netbox.com>`

```
Bob,

This may be very simple.  If you are in an edit or view dialog, an edit macro
will do exactly what you want.  I have one that I'll post here.  If you want,
I'll email the macro as a text file that you can upload.

Essentially I have two: One for edit (E) and one for browse (B).  Because it's
an edit macro (which works in View mode, not Browse), you can be editting a
member/dataset session and type the command (E or B in this case).

You'll have to take a look it's not in CLIST, it's REXX source and should be
added to a dataset allocated as SYSUPROC.  It's even documented and has usage
help available typing "E ?".

Good luck.  I hope this helps!

********* Start of paste **************
/* REXX */
/*

   Edit another member in the same or another dataset.
   Does not disturb the current edit session. Current session
   resumes after subsequent edit session.

   This macro works in Panvalet edit as well as ISPF edit, however
   you cannot try to edit a panlib, for obvious reasons.

   Syntax:

       To edit a member in the same dataset: "E member"
       To edit a member in another dataset: "E node.node(mbr)"
       To get a list of members in a dataset: "E node.node"

       NOTE: If high level qualifier of dsn is not the same as
             the ISPF Profile Prefix setting, the dsn should be
             enclosed in apostrophes: E 'QUAL.FNTST.SOURCE'.

   Assumptions:

     - First node of dsn has been omitted and will be replaced by
       the TSO Profile Prefix unless dsn is enclosed in aposts.
     - If only one node of dsn has been passed, we build the second
       node using the assumption that the second node is users initials.
     - Standard dataset names are used for datasets;  there are three
       nodes in the dsn, such as "DEVL.GPC.EMAC", "DEVL.GPC.REXX", etc.

   GPC - 2/04/95
*/
arg parm
address ISPEXEC
"ISREDIT MACRO PROCESS (PARM)"  /* we be a macro         */
address ISPEXEC "VGET ZUSER"      /* get userid            */

if parm ="?" then do
  say "Usage: e "
  say "       e member"
  say "or:    e q3(member)"
  say "or:    e q2.q3(member)"
  say "or:    e q1.q2.q3(member)"
  say "or:    e 'q1.q2.q3...qn(member)'"
  say "                          "
  say "This edit macro assumes your tso profile PREFIX setting matches"
  say "high level qualifier of the target dataset.  If this is not "
  say "true you must enclose the fully qualified dsn in apostrophies."
  say "                          "
  say "e (no argument) displays a member list of current dataset."
  say "e member works if member is in same dataset being editted."
  say "e q3(member) works when 1st and 2nd nodes match current dsn."
  say "e q2.q3(member) works when 1st, 2nd and 3rd node match dsn."
  say "dsn(member) in apostrophes work with any dataset."
  say "                          "
  say "                          "
  exit 4
  end

user=zuser                        /* save it               */
initials="RIGHT"(user,3)          /* get user initials     */
if initials="GXC" then initials='GPC'
/*
   If a dsn was used, we'll find periods separating the nodes.
 */

if "POS"(".",parm)>0 then do      /* have nodes, ie dsn?   */
  if "POS"("(",parm)>0 then do    /* have member too?      */
    parse var parm ds "(" member ")" . . /* get components */
    eparm=parm
    DOEDIT(EPARM)
    end
  else do                         /* no mbr, must be flat  */
    eparm=parm
    DOEDIT(eparm)
    end
  end

/*
   If we get here we have one node and a member, or we simply want
   an msl of the members in the current dataset.

   If the former, tack on the initials as the 2nd node of the dsn.
 */

if "POS"("(",parm)>0 then do            /* have member too?    */
  parse var parm ds "(" member ")" . .  /* break it up         */
  eparm=initials��"."��parm             /* add the second node */
  DOEDIT(eparm)
  end
else do                                 /* just member, must be seq */
  address ISREDIT "(ds)=DATASET"        /* current dsn              */
  parse var ds ."."n2"."n3 rest
  ds=n2��"."��n3��rest
  if parm<>"" then do                   /* parm must be member      */
    eparm=ds��"("��parm��")"            /* build dsn(mbr)           */
    DOEDIT(eparm)
    end
  else do                               /* no parms, just an msl    */
    eparm=ds
    DOEDIT(eparm)
    end
  end

DOEDIT:
arg eparm
tparm="TRANSLATE"(eparm," ",".") /* Remove the periods */
parse var tparm n1 n2 n3  /* Probable, if high lvl qual="PANVALET" */
if n1="PANVALET" then do
  ZEDLMSG="When in PVEDIT you must specify a PDS dataset node and member."
  "SETMSG MSG(ISRZ001)"
  exit 4
  end
x="SYSDSN"(eparm)
if x<>"OK" & x<>"MEMBER NOT FOUND" then do /* can create new member */
  ZEDLMSG = x ��"<" eparm ">"
  "SETMSG MSG(ISRZ001)"
  exit 4
  end
  address ISPEXEC "EDIT DATASET("eparm")"


************* End of Paste

-----== Posted via Deja News, The Leader in Internet Discussion ==-----
http://www.dejanews.com/rg_mkgrp.xp   Create Your Own Free Member Forum

##### ↳ ↳ Re: TSO ISPF, CLISTs and/or REXX exec

- **From:** "Chomuk Family" <gpchomuk@wwa.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01bdd17e$7f4a4460$1e3ef1cf@Pchomuk>`
- **References:** `<35DB9C35.4B24@netbox.com> <6s1q6q$1t4$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <35E4740A.1375@beyond-software.com>`

```
The macro saves me a heck of alot of typing time and lessens the chance of
fat-fingering dataset names.

I should point out that I use edit macros just to avoid having to type TSO
..
Example: an edit macro that I call JES, allows me to type JES H from the
edit command line.  It issues the TSO SDSF command rather than using, say
"=s" to get to SDSF.

Most sites have some sort of dataset naming standards.  The edit macro,
with modifications to match dataset naming conventions, allow you to omit
higher level nodes of the dataset name.  I try to stick to a simple naming
standard for my personal and project dataset names. 

Example. our project team uses standard, three node dsns and all datasets
have high-level-qualifier (hlq) of  YYYY.  Therefore, if all I ever do is
edit/view datasets that start with YYYY, the macro allows me now to never
have to type it again at the command line; it's assumed.  

Further, for my datasets I use my initials for the second qualifier.  If
all I ever worked were my own datasets, all those starting with "DEVL.GPC",
the macro allows me to never have to type "DEVL.GPC".  It's assumed.

Edit supports a subset of what the edit macro performs.  Edit does allow
recursive edits. EDIT member on the command line will work.  EDIT
'fully.qualified.dsn(member)' works also.  Edit w/no argument brings up the
edit dialog.

Assume three datasets exist and are:

	groupa.usera.jcl
	groupa.usera.rexx
	groupa.usera.emac

During an edit session on any dataset, the macro would allow you to enter
"E jcl(imaspzap)".  The macro will assume dataset name to be
group.user.jcl(imaspzap).

Similarly I could type "e data(member)".   Hope this help at least
understand the macro.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: TSO ISPF, CLISTs and/or REXX exec

- **From:** gchomuk@my-dejanews.com
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6s45ab$oro$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`
- **References:** `<35DB9C35.4B24@netbox.com> <6s1q6q$1t4$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <35E4740A.1375@beyond-software.com>`

```
ISPF supports the edit membername or edit "fully.qualified.dataset(name)".

This macro fills in high level qualifiers so they don't have to be typed in.
For example assume the following pds datasets exist:

project.groupa.typea
project.groupa.typeb
project.groupb.whatever

While in browse or view, I can issue:

- "e typea(member)" the macro ensures that the dsn is expanded to
Project.groupa.typea(member).

I also use edit macros to front-end rexx procedures.  For example, I have
another macro that I call "JES" that invokes sdsf, sets the prefix and you can
also to specify which queue to start in.  Example:  "JES H"  displays the held
queue and sets the prefix.  The prefix needs to be set because sdsf invoked
this way does not profile this information.  Another way of doing this would
have been issue TSO SDSF, but you  lose the ability to swap to your split
screen.

Here's the (much smaller) rexx procedure I call JES:

This also demonastrate A way to start a rexx procedure without having to issue
TSO.

/* rexx */
/*
   Macro to start SDSF and go directly to the requested queue.
   gpc - 1/15/95
*/
parse upper arg q prefix
"ISREDIT MACRO NOPROCESS JES (Q PREFIX)"
address ISPEXEC
doprefix=0
if prefix<>"" then doprefix=1
if doprefix then do
  if 'LEFT'(prefix,3)="IMS" then pc="pre imstreg*";
  else pc="pre d966gpc"
end /* doprefix */
/*"&ZCMD = 'REM'"*/
/*.resp = enter  */
"SELECT PGM(ISFISP) NOCHECK NEWAPPL(ISF) PARM("Q")"
**************************** Bottom of Data *********************

the simplest solution is to use a RECORD sequential file with

(I think).

#### ↳ Re: Libraries (and/vs) Native Language support

- **From:** "Warren G. Simmons" <warrens@inficad.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E4A194.24FA@inficad.com>`
- **References:** `<35D84D07.C072827@my-dejanews.com> <2CE7C7444FFB0872.B5BC49FF7D71D33E.26E4410B363C94BC@library-proxy.airnews.net> <6rbsgf$8b$3@news.enterprise.net> <35D98E0D.DCB61648@my-dejanews.com> <stanw-1808981531530001@ts2-96.the-wire.com> <6rk6cj$3qp$1@news.goodnet.com> <6s20hi$as4$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> 
> In both the Perl and C discussions comparing them to COBOL, there have been
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> libraries.

Your discussion of these points puts into words in a way I never could
have achieved. Wm's points hit me where I live. It is emotional with me.
My whole relationship with COBOL has been to create a language that
covers our needs. Many have put off some of those needs as not Business
Oriented. This almays made me wonder just what Business means to them.
Yet, I found the interface to something like the sort/mearge quite
easy to accept because (as it seemed to me) those kinds of software
often included entry points, and if not could be "purchased". 

Of all the qualities of a computer language that people prefer, the one
that makes most sence to invest in for the long haul is something you
can read, and has or developes a common meaning. Otherwise, down the
line, not only do you have to either do it over, but you may have to
learn something which has no known meaning. Perhaps for those who lived
during the time this was in use, but for those that follow, a clear
statement of the problem solution is the best spent effort. It was a
surprise that enough English is spoken around the world for COBOL to
catch on so much. It is more a surprise to me to have such well thought 
out ideas about the result of the actions of a COMMITTEE! Still, if
Dilbert is right, many who read and write and speak do not yet
understand "the language". Bosses who permit some of the things you
people sometimes write about are surely blind, deaf, and a few yards
short of .....!  

If a windows type interface (maybe it's a gui) is a universal
requirement, and the features of this gui are destined to become part of
the English Language (and they seem to be doing that) then Big Bill for
the good of his country should put the specs in the public domain, and
publish for all to view the specs of all the dll's in Windows for ever.
Otherwise, (perhaps else) another method might be found. 

The GUI is great for creating an industry of specialist who create
more and more tools that do less and less. Kind of a lego world. How
many features of the Win GUI are really used in every day production
programs? Just what would it take to spec a data division Window or WI
record description?  Perhaps we are waiting to see how voice I/O will
change things!

I really enjoy the exchanges you people have at this level. 

Keep it up, please.

Warren Simmons
```

<a id="t-4213dbd21c"></a>
## Thread: Libraries (and/vs) Native Language support — 1 msgs

### Libraries (and/vs) Native Language support

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6s1u0s$6vp@dfw-ixnews8.ix.netcom.com>`

```
In both the Perl and C discussions comparing them to COBOL, there have been
some side comments about "libraries" vs native language support for certain
features.  One post explicitly said something about how "stupid" it would be
to put some things in the native language that could/should be in callable
libraries.

This is an "interesting" debate and one that I think is "emotional" with
minimal chance of ever convincing a believer in one that he/she should
change to the other.

For example (and there are MANY other examples too), one of the "strengths"
of COBOL is its native language support for "Indexed files" - and this
support is required for any COBOL compiler and run-time (that claims to be
Standard) - regardless of what products or operating system support there is
for "indexed files" outside of COBOL.  Furthermore, that language is truly
portable and MUST work the same regardless of what product (COBOL, operating
system, or separate file product) is providing the underlying support. To
most COBOL advocates this is a real advantage and is much better than
requiring a separate library for such file handling (much less a separate
library for accessing each different type of indexed file).

On the other hand, today (and in the next Standard), Standard COBOL relies
on a call interface to provide GUI/Windowing screen interfaces.  Some
implementers have added native language for this (most notably AcuCOBOL) but
most have not.  One of the places that COBOL is changing between '85 and
2000 is in providing Standard support for all the features needed to access
such API's - no matter what system they were originally designed for.  (This
includes adding standard pointers, procedure-pointers, return by value,
recursion, etc).  In this way it is hoped/expected that COBOL applications
will be able to take advantage of any library of callable routines that can
be used by other languages.

There is, however, still a question about when new native language should be
added vs when a call interface is acceptable.  In the history and design of
COBOL, the general (with some notable exceptions) guiding principal has been
to ADD native language when it is expected that the same "functionality"
will be desirable in a wide variety of applications and platforms and to
rely on outside API's when the interfaces are to operating specific or
platform specific features.

Bottom-Line: If you think that having minimal native language is best and
using callable libraries (APIs)  is the way to go, that is a reasonable
view - for a language that has grown up that way.  However, the goal of
COBOL is to provide the most portable source code that separates the
application logic from the environmentally specific implementations of
universally available functionality.

P.S. I have not mentioned the arguments of what you need to do when your
"interface software" changes, for example from one ISAM product to another.
Do you recompile? re-link-edit? re-bind? re-something else? run with a
different run-time library?  Again, I don't think I will ever convince
people who are used to one to that the other is better?
```

---

## Thread: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl) — 1 msgs

### Re: Writing device drivers in C (was: Re: COBOL and Perl)

- **From:** paulr@bix.com (paulr)
- **Date:** 1998-08-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.c
- **Message-ID:** `<6s2474$hf2@lotho.delphi.com>`
- **References:** `<35e08cf0.3273969@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <3298232.76073.22283@kcbbs.gen.nz> <35df95c8.636306@nntp.ix.netcom.com> <I1%D1.448$Kt4.2131068@news1.atlantic.net> <6rqiq9$ehg@lotho.delphi.com> <ZYdE1.473$Kt4.2409046@news1.atlantic.net> <6s007l$4u2@lotho..delphi.com> <904129648snz@genesis.demon.co.uk> <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com> <6s1fsg$2io@news-central.tiac.net>`

```
Wups, you got me on that one Martin- does it make sense 
to say Indexed files instead of ISAM? I must admit, 
I do put the two together, since ISAM is a method
to me and not a product. :) 

-Paul


Martin Ambuhl (mambuhl@tiac.net) wrote:
: paulr wrote in message <6s106c$cke@lotho.delphi.com>...
: |
: |Lawrence,
: |  Don't take this as an argument, because it isn't, but separating C
: |from platform libraries is like separating COBOL from ISAM. They
: |simply have to go together.
: ======
: I find this fascinating, because I was on the team that built the first
: COBOL compiler for the PDP-10 and therefore paid a lot of attention to
: the development of COBOL implementations in the late 60s and early 70s
: for major vendors.  I remember all too well the vast amount of code
: written in COBOL before IBM or DEC either introduced ISAM as part of
: their COBOL implementations.

: There are basically two cases
: 1) ISAM is standardized and portable.  This case corresponds to using
: standard libraries in C.  It would be perfectly possible to make
: comments about COBOL or C in such a case.
: 2) ISAM is not standardized and not portable.  This case corresponds to
: using platform-specific libraries in C.  It is impossible in this case
: to make widely appropriate comments about either COBOL or C.
```

---

John Porter (jdporter@min.net) wrote:
: Bob Wolfe wrote:
: > 
: > It is certainly possible
: > to employ the use of an ANSI standard CALL USING statement and pass
: > parameters to an external screen handling routine.
: > 
: > Although the screen handling routine itself may be written using
: > direct WINAPI calls or C, the COBOL program is still controlling the
: > screen handling with ANSI standard code.  As long as the external
: > screen handling module is written to support parameter passing by
: > various compilers, the COBOL source code will indeed work with many
: > different compilers.  There are several COBOL screen handling tools
: > which take this approach.

: Cool!  Does this mean that there is actually a standard
: interface (calling protocol), and that how the external
: module chooses to implement it -- text screen, GUI window --
: is transparent to the calling program?  So that you could
: swap one external module for another, getting different
: visual results, but without changing the calling code at
: all?  If so, that is extremely cool.

: -- 
: John Porter
```

Bill Lynch wrote:
> 
> Charles F Hankel wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> problem solvers, no two ways about it.

And you learn pretty quickly not to use words in the DSnames that can
cause you instant problems like BACKUP, BKUP, BKP.  At a couple of sites
anything with any name resembling "backup" was archived within half an
hour at the most.  Damned annoying when you make a safety backup, the
job falls over and you need a quick restore.  More than once, I have
lost a backup between steps because the job ran at the same time as SMS
was hunting backups.

I will admit to feeling sorry for the tape robots, though.  After all,
they were only doing their job and didn't ask to be there in the way;
they just wanted a quiet life... :-)
```

## Thread: Help needed with COBOL program. — 23 this month / 26 total

### Help needed with COBOL program.

- **From:** RICHARD ELDRIDGE <eldridge@coventry.ac.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00

```

I am writing a system for a pub and have a small problem which I hope one 
of you can help me with :)

I have an indexed file which is structured as follows:

    FD STAFF-FILE

	DATA RECORD IS STAFF-FILE-RECORD.
    01 STAFF-FILE-RECORD.
	03 CODE-NUMBER		    PIC 999.
	03 STAFF-NAME		    PIC X(20).
	03 TAKINGS-THIS-SESSION	    PIC PIC 9999V99.
    

When the system is exited I want the "TAKINGS-THIS-SESSION" field to be 
set back to 0000.00 for ALL of the records on the file.

Can anyone suggest the code to achieve this as I have not been able to 
work it out yet.

Thanks........

#### ↳ Re: Help needed with COBOL program.

- **From:** "Dennis J. Minette" <dennis_minette@email.msn.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<O21H2jf09GA.126@upnetnews05>`
- **References:** `<Pine.OSF.3.91.980827145459.24584A-100000@godiva>`

```
Working for ale, eh?  This sounds a lot like homework.

At the end of your COBOL program:

    CLOSE STAFF-FILE.
    OPEN I-O  STAFF-FILE.
    READ STAFF-FILE NEXT RECORD
        AT END MOVE HIGH-VALUES TO STAFF-NAME.
    PERFORM UNTIL STAFF-NAME = HIGH-VALUES
        MOVE ZEROES TO TAKINGS-THIS-SESSION
        REWRITE STAFF-FILE-RECORD
   *      include invalid key clause here if this is an ISAM file
        READ STAFF-FILE NEXT RECORD
             AT END MOVE HIGH-VALUES TO STAFF-NAME
        END-READ
    END-PERFORM.
    CLOSE STAFF-FILE.
    STOP RUN.

Thane, I'm bothered by the high-value trick.
Why not simply:

     CLOSE STAFF-FILE
     OPEN I-O  STAFF-FILE
     Perform until Staff-File-status-1 not = zero
           Read Staff-File Next Record
            If Staff-File-status-1 = zero
                  Move Zeros to Takings-This-Season
                  Rewrite Staff-File-Record
           End-If
     End-Perform
     CLOSE STAFF-FILE
     STOP RUN
     .

This also tests the result of the open and the rewrite.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Help needed with COBOL program.

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3298239.73449.13132@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<6s5oq7$bmh$1@o.online.no>`

```
In message <<6s5oq7$bmh$1@o.online.no>> "Erlend Moen" <emoen@hotmail.com> writes:
> 
> 
> Couldn't agree more!
> But - what's the purpose of the "high-value trick" at all?
> I've never done this in any of my programs.


In the particular example given the whole file was being read
and so using file status to end the processing is reasonable.

However, most of the time only part of a file is being 
processed and so it is slightly more complex.  For example,
all the lines of an order, all the transactions for a
customer, etc:

        MOVE Order-Number TO OrderLine-Key
        START OrderLine-File
            KEY NOT < OrderLine-Key
             INVALID KEY
                 MOVE HIGH-VALUES TO OrderLine-Key
        END-START
        PERFORM
            UNTIL OrderLine-Order NOT = Order-Number

             READ OrderLine-File
                 AT END
                      MOVE HIGH-VALUES TO OrderLine-Key
                 NOT AT END
                 IF OrderLine-Order = Order-Number
                      ......

This caters for the order being the highest number on file
and having no lines (will give invalid key on start) and
for last having lines and any embedded order.

It is not possible to use just the file status because
it needs to stop before end-of-file, and because there
may be, say, a record lock (you do allow for file
sharing don't you ?).  Also because START and READ will
not change the record when the fail (INVALID KEY or
AT END) it is not enough to look for change in key by
itself.

Now it would work if you used NOT INVALID KEY on the
START to avoid processing when that fails, and then
doing nothing in the AT END and using file status to
terminate the perform, but then it will read to
physical end of file each time.

This may not be noticed with test data (short file) or
when order numbers are being added at the end as there
will only be a small overhead each time because only
a small numbers of orders have been added.  But if 
the number is ever reset the processed time will 
suddenly blow out.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Help needed with COBOL program.

- **From:** redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)

- **Message-ID:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-7qfOztl06Sgx@Dwight_Miller.iix.com>`
- **References:** `<Pine.OSF.3.91.980827145459.24584A-100000@godiva> <O21H2jf09GA.126@upnetnews05> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-Pz1FxCPHRKLJ@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <35e5de94.0@news1.ibm.net> <6s5oq7$bmh$1@o.online.no>`

```
On Fri, 28 Aug 1998 08:12:46, "Erlend Moen" <emoen@hotmail.com> wrote:
> Couldn't agree more!
> But - what's the purpose of the "high-value trick" at all?
> I've never done this in any of my programs.
> 

It can be used in sequential updates.  When you reach the end of one 
file, in the merge/update process, set your "compare" field to high 
values, the program logic can remain clean, and no further reads 
against this file will be attempted.
```

#### ↳ Re: Help needed with COBOL program.

- **From:** "COBOL Frog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E71AF6.6CCBAC42@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<Pine.OSF.3.91.980827145459.24584A-100000@godiva>`

```
At first RICHARD ELDRIDGE wrote:

>     FD STAFF-FILE
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>         03 STAFF-NAME               PIC X(20).

Then Dennis J. Minette wrote:

>     READ STAFF-FILE NEXT RECORD
>         AT END MOVE HIGH-VALUES TO STAFF-NAME.

Likewise Thane Hubbell wrote in lower case:

>            Read Staff-File Next Record
>                At End
>                   Move High-Values to Staff-Name

The Cobol Standard wrote something like:

When AT END occurs, the contents of the record area are undefined. In practice I
have worked on computers where this meant that the record area was pointing at an
undefined location of memory and thus would a MOVE HIGH-VALUE (or whatever) TO
whateverFieldInThatArea abnormally end the program. s0C4. Invalid. Error. Boommm.

Better define some working-storage swith/flag?

The COBOL Frog
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Help needed with COBOL program.

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "COBOL Frog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35EB06E7.42AABB99@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<Pine.OSF.3.91.980827145459.24584A-100000@godiva> <35E71AF6.6CCBAC42@IMN.nl> <6s7il3$ron$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <35EAADEF.E9F0185D@zip.com.au>`

```
Ken Foskey wrote:

> I beleive that the record area for a file is static only while it is
> open.  I did think about the green fellow's response and fundamentally
…[46 more quoted lines elided]…
> > http://www.dejanews.com/rg_mkgrp.xp   Create Your Own Free Member Forum

Indeed the area is static after AT END but only until CLOSE! My experience is that a lot
further in time / in the program again some statement would like to test for EOF and thus
reference some data area that is garantueed to be stastic even after closure.
working-storage is that static.

I think/recall in particular programs processing in one run a mster file together with
one or more files containing updates to the master. (balance-line mechanism using key
compares). In this kind of programs it is quite common to define a working-storage copy
of the key (sorted)fields together with the EOF flag. Like (3 key fields):

01  currentSituationForFileMaster.
    03  csm-upto-field3.
        05  csm-upto-field2.
            07  csm-upto-field1.
                09  csm-eof      pic x  value zero.
                    88  eof-FileXXX     value "1".
                09  csm-field1   pic x(..).
            07  csm-field2       pic x(..).
        05  csm-field3           pic x(..).

01  previousSituationForFileMaster.
    03  psm-upto-field3.
        05  psm-upto-field2.
            07  psm-upto-field1.
                09  psm-eof      pic x  value zero.
                09  psm-field1   pic x(..).
            07  psm-field2       pic x(..).
        05  psm-field3           pic x(..).

Such a set is there for each update file too. O, you probably all know the trick.

Anyway: at all and every time you can without any harm reference each file-situation.

The read-routine of each sequentially accessed file with any key/sort order in any real
world program I always wrote/write is as such:

ReadFileXXX.
    Move currentSituationForFileXXX to previousSituationForFileXXX
    Read FileXXX next record
    At end
        Set eof-FileXXX to true
        Close FileXXX
    Not at end
        Add 1 to recCountXXX
        Move inrec-field1 to csX-field1
        Move inrec-field2 to csX-field2
        Move inrec-field3 to csX-field3
        If (currentSituationForFileXXX <  previousSituationForFileXXX)
*                                      <= if duplicates are not allowed
        Then
            Display "Sequence error on file ... etc at record # " recCountXXX
            Stop run/Call/Perform error handling
        End-if
    End-read

With //.. DD ..,FREE=CLOSE in JCL, this is some routine that always gave me more than it
cost.

At some IBM location (the IBM centre in Uithoorn, The Netherlands) this was THE standard
routine, in COBOL and in PL/1. For good reasons.

Just my experience!
The COBOL Frog
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Help needed with COBOL program.

- **From:** riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1998-08-29T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3298240.3394.27954@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<35E71AF6.6CCBAC42@IMN.nl>`

```
In message <<35E71AF6.6CCBAC42@IMN.nl>> "COBOL Frog  writes:
> 
> The Cobol Standard wrote something like:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Better define some working-storage swith/flag?

This cannot be the case in any sensible implementation.

After reading a file to the end it is perfectly valid to do:

          MOVE SomeOther-Value   TO File-Key
          START SomeFile
              KEY NOT < File-Key
              .....

Your hypothetic 'experience' would have us believe that the
program would crash with this perfectly valid code following
on from a normal ending to the file.

Now the _content_ of the record buffer may well be undefined
after an AT END (and after a WRITE or REWRITE or a failed
operation) but the _existence_ of the record buffer should
never be in doubt.
```

#### ↳ Re: Help needed with COBOL program.

- **From:** bruce.chris@hyder.com

- **Message-ID:** `<6s5jr3$e0o$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`

  RICHARD ELDRIDGE <eldridge@coventry.ac.uk> wrote:
>
> I am writing a system for a pub and have a small problem which I hope one
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> work it out yet.

<snip> ------- You don't say what environment you're running but assuming
it's a disk file you could try (schematic logic follows)

CLOSE the file
OPEN the file I-O
READ the file
DO while not end-of-file
  SET TAKINGS-THIS-SESSION to zero
  REWRITE the record
  READ the file
ENDDO
CLOSE the file

Job done. . .

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

I have read all of the other responses and would go along with the
general tenor except that I would question as to why you need to zero
out the field at the end of processing.

If it is simply a matter of preparing for the next session, then I would
suggest that you initialise it as zero on the read at the start of the
next session - it's so much less messy; and it gives you option of
having the data handy for a program restart in the event of a system
failure at the crucial moment.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COMP-3, S9(8)

```

Wei Lu wrote in message ...
>Could you please tell me that ?
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>4)    MOVE     A          TO     SIGN1.

Yes to all.

>
>What is the size of S9(8)  ?
>What is the size of S9(8) COMP-3 ?


Both  sizes are platform dependant.  S9(8) will usually be 8 bytes,
but can be forced to nine by using a sign separate declaration. On
the odd platform, it will default to 9.  Comp-3 (I may be wrong here
because I do not usually use comp-3) is usually a word, so
in 32 bit platform, it will be four bytes, two's comp.

>
```

On a dEC Alpha  S9(n) COMP-3  is allocated (n +1)/2 bytes, rounded UP, and
is Packed Decimal (of course, n <= 18). So, S9(8) COMP-3 is allocated 5
bytes in the alien dEC netherworld.

Yes, and PIC 9(8) COMP-3 takes the same amount of space, because of a moronic

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COMP-3, S9(8)

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1998-08-29T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3298240.72395.29442@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<NYIF1.3224$sG2.3107700@news3.mia.bellsouth.net>`

```
In message <<NYIF1.3224$sG2.3107700@news3.mia.bellsouth.net>> "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net> writes:
> >
> >comp-3 is also platform dependent.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> that decision has caused.

It may have wasted a few nybbles, but it peobably saved a few
bays full of electronics.

Put another way, to have instructions that operated on signed
or unsigned packed decimal would have required a bit somewhere
to indicate that it had the sign position or not.  This
either required a flag on the field (which took the same
space as the sign itself) or took a position in the
instruction - in ever instruction that worked on packed.

Having the flag in the field neither saves nor wastes space
which is thus indifferent as to whether it is signed or not.
Having the flag in the instruction set 'wastes' a bit in
every instruction.

The total number of wasted bits is either the even digit
unsigned packed values in memory (x4) or the number of
instructions in memory (all else being equal).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COMP-3, S9(8)

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<_38G1.3571$_B3.4008867@news2.mia.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<NYIF1.3224$sG2.3107700@news3.mia.bellsouth.net> <3298240.72395.29442@kcbbs.gen.nz>`

```
Richard Plinston wrote:
>Judson McClendon writes:
>> >
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>instructions in memory (all else being equal).


The instruction format already embodies the field type, or you
would have no way to handle packed BCD, signed and unsigned binary,
and signed and unsigned display numeric.  It would at most take only
one extra bit in the instruction, and that assumes the instruction
format already fully utilizes all the bits in the instruction.  Since
other architectures handle unsigned BCD quite easily, it is obvious
that it is no big deal to do so.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COMP-3, S9(8)

> >unsigned packed values in memory (x4) or the number of
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> one extra bit in the instruction, and that assumes the instruction

So a 33 bit word ?

> format already fully utilizes all the bits in the instruction.  Since
> other architectures handle unsigned BCD quite easily, it is obvious
> that it is no big deal to do so.

Other architectures don't have the same instruction format
and layout (by definition).  Given that the BCD instructions
must have some refernce already to the number of bytes
involved, whereas the binary and floating only require a
single/double flag and a signed/unsigned flag, then the
number of bits required may already fill the 32 bit word.

'stealing' a bit from one of the fields will impose its
own constraints elsewhere, one that 'other architectures'
may lead to other wastages in other formats.

If you actually want to make a point then please do so by
indicating your own redesign of the instruction set to
show exactly where your bit will come from and also 
indicate the disadvantages that this redesign will
create so that a balanced view can be appreciated.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COMP-3, S9(8)

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "COBOL Frog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35EB0D31.B651C570@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<_38G1.3571$_B3.4008867@news2.mia.bellsouth.net> <3298242.27509.4373@kcbbs.gen.nz>`

```
Richard, Judson,

There is no bit needed at all to distinguish between short or lon, fixed or float, signed or unsigned or whatever
distinct type you may think of ...

The compiler knows the type from its definition. This definition is programmer-made by using the correct keywords in
the data declarations: float long fixed, comp-2 etc. etc. (keywords in C / C++ / COBOL / PL/1 / Pascal etc) or
(specific for COBOL and look-alikes) the picture symbols. The that compiler just generates the correct machine
instructions (m/i). This means that those m/i's do NOT look after the type, they are made for just one type. Except
... no even NOT conversion m/i's: even those are predefines to convert from GIVEN type x to type y and do not test if
var1 is indeed type x etc.

The machines that I know that are able to do mathematic with signed and unsigned packed (I mean REALY unsigned in
internal storage, not with a fake absolute sign like IBM) all had machine instructions for both types OR had
conversion instructions.

The COBOL Frog
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COMP-3, S9(8)

- **From:** bruce.chris@hyder.com
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6s5kga$f0s$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`
- **References:** `<6rf6sn$47a@news3.force9.net> <35e2cb3c.5343496@news.apk.net> <35e5e47e.5284114@news1.frb.gov> <Pine.SOL.3.95.980827222852.23903C-100000@me.udel.edu> <6s569k$f95$1@news.igs.net>`

```
In article <6s569k$f95$1@news.igs.net>,
  "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com> wrote:
>
> Wei Lu wrote in message ...
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>

On an IBM mainframe (32 bit) a COMP-3 variable	is generally known as packed
decimal and is stored with each digit in the picture taking half a byte (a
nibble) and the sign taking half a byte. So in the example given the S9(8)
COMP-3 would be (according the above formula) four and half bytes - which is
rounded to five bytes. Hence its alsways a good idea to declare your COMP-3
variables with an odd number of 9s.

Internally it would be stored as 01,23,45,67,8S where S is C (+) D(-) F
(unsigned). Other values are permitted as the sign to but these are most
commonly seen. (Comma between each byte to illustrate!)

The digits are directly readable in a dump; there's no need to do any hex-dec
conversion to see what value the variable has.

-----== Posted via Deja News, The Leader in Internet Discussion ==-----
http://www.dejanews.com/rg_mkgrp.xp   Create Your Own Free Member Forum
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COMP-3, S9(8)

- **From:** Bill Lynch <wblynch@att.net>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E63459.EF060B28@att.net>`
- **References:** `<6rf6sn$47a@news3.force9.net> <35e2cb3c.5343496@news.apk.net> <35e5e47e.5284114@news1.frb.gov> <Pine.SOL.3.95.980827222852.23903C-100000@me.udel.edu>`

```
Wei Lu wrote:
> 
(snip)
> 
> What is the size of S9(8)  ?

With any IBM COBOL compiler I've ever seen (going back to D-level COBOL
in DOS) S9(8) will be 8 bytes (I assume the earlier reply was correct
concerning a separate sign).

> What is the size of S9(8) COMP-3 ?

Again, with any IBM COBOL compiler I've ever seen (going back to D-level
COBOL in DOS) S9(8) COMP-3 will be 5 bytes. COMP-3 is packed and a
packed field *always* has an odd number of bytes (in memory, not
according to COBOL), plus the sign. So your example will need 9 digits
plus the sign, divided by 2 to get the length in bytes. This has nothing
to do with words (the earlier reply was probably thinking of COMP, not
COMP-3, or some other platform), your field will be byte aligned,
nothing more.

If you're using a compiler other an IBM one, you need feedback from
someone else.

Bill Lynch
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COMP-3, S9(8)

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "COBOL Frog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E71D94.CEEA307B@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<6rf6sn$47a@news3.force9.net> <35e2cb3c.5343496@news.apk.net> <35e5e47e.5284114@news1.frb.gov> <Pine.SOL.3.95.980827222852.23903C-100000@me.udel.edu> <35E63459.EF060B28@att.net> <6s5p07$bug$1@o.online.no>`

```
Erlend Moen wrote:

> This goes for Microsoft/MicroFocus and Acucobol as well.

But not for Bull DPS machines.

PIC S9(n) will take (n+1)/2 bytes, rounded up.
So, when n is odd, bytes are fully filled, when n is even, a half byte
is added (at front and kept zero) to make up for whole bytes. So far it
is like IBM harware.

B U T when the picture has NO sign ... things are different:
IBM h/w always stores a sign, thats the "+1" in the above formula. But
Bull h/w/ does not store a sign when not present in the picture. So PIC
9(n) will take n/2 bytes. rounded up.
So when n is EVEN, bytes are fully filled, when n is ODD, a half byte is
added (at front and kept zero) to make up for whole bytes. That is NOT
like IBM harware.

The COBOL Frog
```

the point i am trying to make is declaring data and code

```
G Moore wrote:
> 
> On Thu, 27 Aug 1998 11:48:50 +0100, Charles F Hankel
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> and comments in each assembler is system dependant.

OK.  But is system independence necessarily a good thing?  The sheer
cost of moving between system platforms makes it a bad move in most
circumstances and is a strategy loved only by management consultants and
dumb managers in most circumstances.

As I may heve mentioned on another thread, I have just finished at a
site where I had some involvement in the original main business system
that was developed during the late Sixties and early Seventies.  The
super strategy is to break down this backbone system, converting it into
a variety of flavours of client/server packages on a fancy network of
HP-UX servers and NT and HP-UX workstations.  So far, this project has
been going for three years with quite a lot of people involved and it is
not y2k compliant: the old system has always been y2k compliant.  There
is no real reason to change the backbone other than the dogma that says
that client/server is best and misunderstands what can be achieved by
mainframe systems.  That company is spending a fortune on an unnecessary
exercise and it is Joe Public who ends up paying in (in this case)
higher insurance premiums.
```

In message <<35E53992.B265BC29@hankel.mersinet.co.uk>> Charles F Hankel <charles@hankel.mersinet.co.uk> writes:
> >

'Assembler' (you are actually only referring to a specific 
assembler here) is only 'easier' if it is what you know.  To
a C programmer, C will always be easier.  To someone who
does know a specific assembler and wants a specific set
of instructions executed, and does not know C then, obviously,
"Assembler is so much easier".


However, if you had to write a program for, say, a Sun or even
an Intel box, you would find that it would probably be 'easier'
for you to learn C than to be retrained in assembler.

(No, assemblers are not all like 360, computers are not all like
360, some are savagely different, all are different).

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL and Perl

- **From:** gvwmoore@ix.netcom.com (G Moore)
- **Date:** 1998-08-29T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35ee404f.8809146@nntp.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<35E53992.B265BC29@hankel.mersinet.co.uk> <3298239.19075.16593@kcbbs.gen.nz>`

```
On 28 Aug 98 05:17:55 GMT, riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
wrote:

>
>'Assembler' (you are actually only referring to a specific 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>"Assembler is so much easier".

i took a course in assembler and one in C. i could get the
programs in C to almost work, and my programs in asm would
NEVER work. but the thing i noted most was that I could
port my C code easily to other machines. assembler would
require u to learn a new asm set, a new declaration, ect.

an example:

in openedition C, you could write:

register 
decimal (dollarsandcents,twodecimalplaces = 2) somenumber;

in asm, you would simply use registers 14 and 15 or something.

but if you wanted to port your code, then

in C, you simply change it to a float.

in asm, you would have to change your declaration, and
any code thereafter which references the packed data format.

lets pretend u have to keep your computer up 24/7...
(which is an ok assumption with micros)

in C, you could use TCP/IP easily, and abstract 1 library
from the 2 seperate machines.

but in asm, you would have to use two different libraries
and two different assemblers.

in asm, u are locked into the assembler-maker vendor. period.

in C, you have the option of changing compilers, and
abstracting the source code based on

#ifdef vendor_compiler
interruptfunction()
{
/*written one way*/
}
#enddef
#ifdef other_vendor
interruptfunction()
{
/*written another way*/
}
#enddef

can u do this in assembler? nope.

in C, the low level memory techniques can be abstracted,

by pointing to a pointer which is register class
for indirect addressing, pointing to a normal variable
for direct addressing, and pointing to the address of
an offset to a variable (or using an array) for 
other indexed addressing (or whatever the word is).

in asm, u have to rewrite *everything*.

before u port architectures, port your asm to C.

the one thing which is missing in C that asm has is
instruction cycle timing, but some C products even
handle that today.

gvwmoore@ix.spam.netcom.com to reply remove the spam
```

```
Richard Plinston wrote:
> 
> 'Assembler' (you are actually only referring to a specific
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> "Assembler is so much easier".

Well, I know Assembler and COBOL, oh and C amongst others, and I still
say that on the IBM mainframe, Assembler is the easiest language to use,
bar none.
 
> However, if you had to write a program for, say, a Sun or even
> an Intel box, you would find that it would probably be 'easier'
> for you to learn C than to be retrained in assembler.

Maybe, maybe not.  I have no experience of a Sun box, so refuse to
comment.  As for an Intel box, my preference would be for COBOL.  C is a
devil that one must treat occasionally with but only out of necessity.
 
> (No, assemblers are not all like 360, computers are not all like
> 360, some are savagely different, all are different).

I'm not saying that they are.  What I did say was that anyone who wrote
Assembler langiage programs on a 360 thirty years ago will have no
problem with the Assembler on the latest generation IBM mainframes.  He
will notice the differences but these will be easy to adapt to.  That's
a thirty year span, and it looks aa though it will remain in being for
another thirty years at least - how many other systems demonstrate that
sort of continuity?

As to how easy the Assembler is to learn, I would put it within the
grasp of most people.  I started maintaining Assembler programs within
an hour of being introduced to the language; true, it took a couple of
months before the Damascene experience but then my time was also taken
up with COBOL programming during this period.

With C, you need to know about the system/compiler's data types.  For
instance, how long is a short integer on particular machines?  You need
to be able to ascertain this sort of information before C can genuinely
be what it is vaunted to be.  At least in COBOL, you don't have to worry
about such things, a quick adjustment to the ENVIRONMENT DIVISION and
you're away.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL and Perl

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<KigG1.4390$sG2.4247621@news3.mia.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<35E53992.B265BC29@hankel.mersinet.co.uk> <3298239.19075.16593@kcbbs.gen.nz> <35E95C8B.5A5DCB94@hankel.mersinet.co.uk>`

```
It really, really does depend on the assembly language.  For example,
I once developed a fair proficiency programming a computer sold by
Burroughs in the mid 70's, called the B1100.  This computer was made
by Varian, and the assembly language was little higher than microcode.
It took literally a routine over 1000 lines long to read a punched
card.  It read one column into two bytes.  As I recall, the first byte
contained the top eight punches (hole = 1 bit), and the second byte
contained the bottom four punches.  I doubt that anyone would say that
the assembly language on that machine was 'easier' than COBOL, or
almost any HLL imaginable. :-)
```
