<a id="t-c2643efa7e"></a>
## Thread: COBOL and Perl — 1 msgs

### Re: COBOL and Perl

- **From:** scott@softbase.com
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.perl.misc,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35e57697.0@news.new-era.net>`
- **References:** `<6r7fi0$9a7$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <6rldhj$na5@lotho.delphi.com> <6rr8ge$5a9$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <35E16365.CF280FEC@hankel.mersinet.co.uk> <6rs4dd$3ft$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <35E1ABCE.363550E1@cks.ssd.k12.wa.us> <6ru2dm$639$3@news.enterprise.net>`

```
Shaun C. Murray (scm@enterprise.net) wrote:
> >I know no COBOL, but I work in an environment where I am surrounded by
> >COBOL people.  A 'real world' example: Recently one of these poor COBOL
> >slaves (that's a term of endearment, really) came to me with a extract
> >file from a small database from one of our departments.  He was dismayed
> >that the file format was not fixed width, but tab delineated. 

> I'd be dismayed at your COBOL programmers if that was a valid complaint.  
> Post your perl code and I'm sure the group can do it in less lines in COBOL. 
> Then give it to your PCS and tell him to take a refresher course.

You have to remember:

	1) variable length record "free format" files such as UNIX
	and every OS after UNIX (Windows, OS/2, etc) uses are
	almost unknown on the mainframe. Using them is possible
	("unformatted" files are used for executable code, etc and
	can be user-created) but is poorly supported.

	2) a single COBOL program doesn't exist in isolation -- it
	is part of a system which can include other COBOL programs,
	sort utilities, etc. Even if you rewrote this one program
	in Perl, it wouldn't do you any good. You'd have to 
	rewrite everything.

People who are used to the UNIX file system and all the OSes created
after this file system became widely known just can't appreciate how
bizarre the mainframe really is.

Scott
```

---

<a id="t-93dc4390e0"></a>
## Thread: compare( Network, Relational) DBMSes — 2 this month / 10 total

_Thread started [1999-07](1999-07.md#t-93dc4390e0)._

#### ↳ Re: compare( Network, Relational) DBMSes

- **From:** Patrick Herring <patrick.herring@bt.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.smalltalk,comp.object,comp.object.logic,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E580CF.5EC4@bt.com>`
- **References:** `<01bdccb3$82bbcd00$c0e6e6c8@mnfala> <35DD6728.A4A0EA62@ifi.uio.no> <35E2C60F.1FE1@bt.com> <35E3DE3F.B333437@ifi.uio.no> <35E41E3F.762C@bt.com> <35E5198E.CAEF927B@ifi.uio.no>`

```
Asbjï¿½rn Danielsen wrote:
> 
> Patrick Herring wrote:
> >
> > Asbjï¿½rn Danielsen wrote:
...
> > More or less. In IDMS there is a query tool that has DML & nothing 
> > else (also, if you save a query for repeat execution and then go and 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> operations?

Dunno, there are some IDMS situations which can't be expressed in SQL.
One IDMS project I worked on was designed to keep the possibility of
easy translation into DB2, which meant that various IDMS features had to
be avoided. However, translating SQL into network DML is easy so maybe
you're right.

I think Cullinet, for at the time they still existed, were only
partially aware of the need for an SQL interface. When CA bought them
Cullinet's plans for the next release (11) were dropped (basically a
very high level automatic application generator) and release 12 had an
SQL interface. Everywhere I've worked that had IDMS rel 12+ the SQL
interface has been non-installed due to resource usage... go figure!

...
> > > A relational DBMS uses a high-level or non-procedural DML, meaning 
> > > the DML (in most cases SQL), and the program(s) using the RDBMS do 
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> an RDBMS program) to the next sentence:

But you did say 'the DML... and the program(s)... do not need to
know...' etc. Not sure what you did mean.

> > > The relational model represents an abstraction of the physical
> > > structure, while Network DBMSs models the physical structure of 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> Except the existence of the set.

Which is a logical thing. And in the equivalent SQL program it would be
the existence of a foreign key relation. Same thing?

> > 'Abstracted' doesn't mean 'hide entirely'! In fact I see relational
> > DBMSes as basically being cut-down networked DBMSes with a 4GL 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Well, I have to disagree. I find them conceptually different. 

Indeed they are. You're probably right that the relational scheme
enables clearer conceptualisations of the relationships between data.
There's no doubt that relations have a proper grounding in relational
algebra & so the whole scheme can, potentially, have maths applied to it
for general benefit. However, this is just the interface, actually
designing a DB, whether relational or network is always a matter of
starting with logical relationships & then deciding the best way to
implement them with the structures available, and if data volume is an
issue you can't avoid it by saying it's only the interface that matters.

The theory was always that SQL would make it possible for non-techies to
access their own data, thus freeing them from the need to go through the
DP department all the time. The last time I worked with DB2 we found our
user couldn't get his head round the concept of a file so he couldn't
even enter his SQL statements. So we did it for him...

...
> The abstraction is ofcourse not of the RDBMS, rather on the structure 
> of the data in the database, and yes it does not imply "hide 
> entirely". If it was totally hidden, how could it then be accessed? 
> The only kind of "database system" being able to hide such info are 
> persistent programming languages supporting orthogonal persistence.

I think you're confusing the interface with the database structure, easy
to do in the relational model since the bits of representation that
aren't syntactically identical with the interface are represented by
data values (I'm thinking of key columns mostly), and we often forget
that when a data value is both data and control that the control bit is
just as physical as a pointer in hex.
 
...
> > The oft-quoted mistake I meant is that there is more unnecessary
> > application changing with network DBMSes than relational ones. I 
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> them).

So whence came the view from your page, that programmers of networked
DBs need to know more about the physical representation of the logical
structure than their counterparts in the relational world? And that
network DB designs are more likely than relational ones to evolve
themselves into an un-upgradeable corner because the interface navigates
the DB structure explicitly by named connections rather than implicitly
by foreign key connections? I still don't get it.

I'd have thought it was relationals that have more problems eg you
can't, as far as I know, have a recursive relation with ref integrity
constraints since then you can't insert anything without, potentially,
itself already existing. I've experienced and heard of a big problem in
that it's difficult to have a unique row id (which comes for free in
IDMS from the record's location, which a program can access) as a direct
result of abstracting the interface so much. Also you get lots of data
duplication with foreign keys, which again is unavoidable from using
data values as pointers.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: compare( Network, Relational) DBMSes

- **From:** "Asbj���rn Danielsen" <asbjornd@ifi.uio.no>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.smalltalk,comp.object,comp.object.logic,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E67297.5170397F@ifi.uio.no>`
- **References:** `<01bdccb3$82bbcd00$c0e6e6c8@mnfala> <35DD6728.A4A0EA62@ifi.uio.no> <35E2C60F.1FE1@bt.com> <35E3DE3F.B333437@ifi.uio.no> <35E41E3F.762C@bt.com> <35E5198E.CAEF927B@ifi.uio.no> <35E580CF.5EC4@bt.com>`

```
Patrick Herring wrote:

> Asbjï¿½rn Danielsen wrote:
> >
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> the existence of a foreign key relation. Same thing?

No, not really. The set is accessed explicit (physical) when used in an
application using network DBMS. In a relational DBMS the foreign key is
only defined when the table is defined and is not used by the application
explicit, though the programmer should ofcourse know about it. As a
consequence the "foreign key" in network DBMSs are logical and physical to
both application and database, in relational DBMSs the foreign key is
logical to the application and logical/physical to the database.

> > > 'Abstracted' doesn't mean 'hide entirely'! In fact I see relational
> > > DBMSes as basically being cut-down networked DBMSes with a 4GL
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Agreed, the implementation is something quite different. E.g. Chris Date
claims that none of the RDBMS vendors have implemented the relational model
as defined by Codd. Take a look at "The Third Manifesto" by Darwen & Date,
available at
http://cdr.cs.uiuc.edu/cgi-bin/cred/security/credentials/record/95-3/papers/manifesto.ps,
or Date being interviewed by DBMS Magazine in October '94 at
http://www.dbmsmag.com/int9410.html, last section "What is the greatest
misconception about relational technology?".

> The theory was always that SQL would make it possible for non-techies to
> access their own data, thus freeing them from the need to go through the
> DP department all the time. The last time I worked with DB2 we found our
> user couldn't get his head round the concept of a file so he couldn't
> even enter his SQL statements. So we did it for him...

:-)No, I think your user represent the common user. Take a person, not
familiar or understanding the relational model, but able to write
syntacticly correct SQL-statements, and in a matter of mininutes he will be
able to create a query (using two or more tables) resulting in a cartesian
product. At least this is my experience :-))

> ...
> So whence came the view from your page, that programmers of networked DBs
> need to know more about the physical representation of the logical
> structure than their counterparts in the relational world?

I haven't said much about the logical structure of a network DBMS. In the
essay I merly present the mechanism of navigation in a network DBMS. The
only thing I mention is that whenever the structure of the database is
changed (meaning e.g. new sets added), the application need to be changed.
For relational DBMSs I say:

"The relational approach separates the program from the physical
implementation of the database, making the program less sensitive to
changes of the physical representation of the data by unifying data and
metadata in the database. This makes the development of programs more
effective and less dependent on changes in the physical representation of
data ".

Because metadata is not "programmed" in both application and database using
the relational model, the code needed, in the program, to be able to access
the data in the database and map them into memory of the application, is
bigger (not necessarily more complex) using a networked DBMS versus a
relational DBMS.
E.g. If you in a network DBMS access a record, and on this record you are
using a set to access a number of other records, you would end up with
something two or three explicit calls to the database and perform the calls
in some kind of controlled loop. In the relational model you would write
one explicit call, a query, and the DBMS would execute it (hiding how the
actual accesses to the data are performed). To put it in one sentence; It
is easier/shorter (for most people, including application developers) to
tell what they want, than expressing the algorithm to be used to achieve
what they want.

> And that network DB designs are more likely than relational ones to
> evolve themselves into an un-upgradeable corner because the interface
> navigates the DB structure explicitly by named connections rather than
> implicitly by foreign key connections? I still don't get it.

Well, to be honest, neither do I. I have never expressed such a view
neither verbally nor in any written form (e.g. the "Essay"). Any database
using any model may get into problems if the model is "wrong" (the only
problem is to find out what "wrong" is. AFAIK no metric exist to detect a
right or wrong data model, only "rules" on how things should be done to get
a "good" model). The most common reason for a model to become "wrong" are
changing requirements. If some DBMS-concept should be able to handle such
changing requirements well, I would suspect it to be network DBMS and
OODBMSs. They would prbably handle such changes better than RDBMSs and
hierarchical DBMSs, due to the fact that their data-models are able to
express relations which the others can't.

If you find any expressions in my Essay suggesting/implying that the
networked data-model are more likely to evolve into an un-upgradeable
corner or that the network data-model is less flexible than some other,
please let me know, because it have never been intended (if it exist in the
Essay, which I find hard to believe)
```

```
Rick Smith wrote:
> 
> Charles F Hankel wrote in message
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> By definition, to be ANSI(/ISO) C, the language must be fully implemented!

But how many C compilers fail to implement the standard?  In
conversation with the BSI (that's what your ANSI is the equivalent of),
I was told that they find that very few compilers properly conform to
the standard.
 
> >Well, I am arrogantly talking from a mainframe perspective here, and I
> >will state again that assembler is easier than C by a country mile and
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

In the main I mean the IBM/etc mainframes though I have written in NAL
on Honeywell/etc series 6000 systems as well.  Both are easy.

Perhaps there could be some confusion caused by the fact that there's a
lot of systems out there that their manufacturers call "mainframes"
which really don't fall into that classification as far as I, and many
others are concerned.  It would seem that some are called mainframes
because they are aiming to take market share from genuine mainframes.

the mainframes'.
 
<...snipped a load because of limited time...>

> >The mainframe environment has a better systems discipline and more
> >commonly accepted methods (such as standard linkage convention) than the
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> ordering, but this was removed in ISO C, 1989.)

Of course they're defined by the operating environment.  In many
operating environments where C is dominant, those conventions simply do
not exist.  It seems that, in such environments, any interoperation
between programs whose source language is different means that the
programmer has to be conscious of the source language used to write the
program that he is calling, and this really should be an irrelevancy for
the programmer.

IMO this probably contributes to the attitude that prevails in the
one-language-for-all harangues that we sometimes see..
```

```
now tend to use HLASM today, though not necessarily exclusively.  The
```

## Thread: COBOL and Perl — 1 msgs

### Re: COBOL and Perl

- **From:** Charles F Hankel <charles@hankel.mersinet.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-08-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.perl.misc,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E55807.4D7AEFDC@hankel.mersinet.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<6r7fi0$9a7$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <6rldhj$na5@lotho.delphi.com> <6rr8ge$5a9$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <35E16365.CF280FEC@hankel.mersinet.co.uk> <35e2fc1f.12756253@nntpd.databasix.com>`

```
Gary L. Burnore wrote:
> 
> I always thought of Selcopy as a language.

Going by the term described elsewhere on this thread, I'm now inclined
to agree with you.  There's a couple of things that I really like about
Selcopy apart from its ease of use.

First, CBL have never tried to promote it beyond its original purpose,
as a tool for programmers that is very easy to use for those one-off
tasks.  Of course, many of us know of Selcopy being used in the live
environment and sometimes in ways that the product's designers did not
originally envisage.  However, it still remains what it was, without
pretensions to "higher" things, unlike some of its competitor products
like Easytrieve.

Second, AFAIK it's not yet been bought up by Computer Associates and
been rebadged to make it look as though CA is a really innovative
company.

> It's comparing apples to oranges.  I've heard for nearly 15 years that COBOL
> is a dying language.  It'll be here for a long time to come as it works and
> it's easy to learn and some places refuse to give it up.  At one bank, I made
> the mistake of referring to one of the COBOL based databases as a legacy
> system.  I only said it once.

Living life on the edge can be dangerous.  Apparently, the new trendy
term is "heritage" systems.  Soon, there'll be listed program status and
national program status that will allow these things to be saved for the
nation.
```

## Thread: Fujitsu COBOL problems — 8 this month / 18 total

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### Fujitsu COBOL problems

- **From:** "Robert Kovacic" <rjk@sbsoft.com.au>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35e63d5f.0@139.134.5.33>`

```
We seem to be unable to contact Fujitsu via their e-mail address for the
last two days ("recipient name not recognized"). Can anyone help with the
following please?

1. When data is entered into a numeric field in the Screen Section the keyed
numbers are accepted into the LEFT end of the field instead of the right.
eg. if the value 23 is entered into a 9(4) field then the result is 2300
instead of 23 which is what all our users are accustomed to. It seems the
only way to force the value to be accepted as 23 is to enter 23. (ie 23
followed by a full stop).

2. How do we stop the ENTER key from acting as a Terminator key when
accepting data into fields in a Screen Section? ie. our users expect the
Enter to move to the next field (like Tab) and NOT to terminate the ACCEPT.
We have already used a keyboard definition file to remap the Function Keys
to return values which suit our code, but there does not seem to be any way
to remap the ENTER key in this manner.

Regards, Robert.
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu COBOL problems

- **From:** redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-rd16cdIcqVxj@Dwight_Miller.iix.com>`
- **References:** `<35e63d5f.0@139.134.5.33>`

```
On Fri, 28 Aug 1998 05:19:00, "Robert Kovacic" <rjk@sbsoft.com.au> 
wrote:

> We seem to be unable to contact Fujitsu via their e-mail address for the
> last two days ("recipient name not recognized"). Can anyone help with the
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

In the screen section define the field as:

03  Screen-Item Line 01 Column 01 Pic Z(4) Using Numeric-Field.

> 2. How do we stop the ENTER key from acting as a Terminator key when
> accepting data into fields in a Screen Section? ie. our users expect the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

You can't.  In MicroFocus you could REDEFINE Enter to be Tab, but I 
never found a way to do that with Fujitsu.  You can't do it on the DEC
either - or any of the compilers that comply with the X-Open screen 
section.  But there IS a solution.

You can DETECT the enter key after the accept, using the CURSOR 
special name, find the cursor position, then set it at the position of
the next field and reissue the accept.
```

```
There are two problems with FSC's ADIS:

- Left (vs right in MF) adjustment of accepted data items.
  This seems to be mostly the differnce between fixed format
entry of MF
  and free format entry of FSC. I was not able to find fixed
format entry 
  in FSC manuals.

- Enter key termiantes the ACCEPT, while in MF it somehow
(don't know how)
  it may act as a TAB key.

There are no easy solutions of these problems that I know
of.
Fujitsu may decide just to introduce the additional
functionality
into ADIS to be more compatible with MF, but I am not the
one
to speculate on what will happen.

As far as our mf2fsc converter goes, we would take any
suggestion
on better migration of MF's ADIS semantics to FSC and
implement it.


Regards,
Vadim Maslov
```

## Thread: IBM mainframe COBOL - how to close an output file and reopen it with a different DSN — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: IBM mainframe COBOL - how to close an output file and reopen it with a different DSN

- **From:** kleidner@earthlink.net (Ken Leidner)
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6s7dfq$31b$1@ash.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`
- **References:** `<6s0p5r$4v0$1@holly.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`

```
While several people said use SVC 99, here is a Cobol only answer.
I tested this on PP 5688-197 IBM SAA AD/Cycle COBOL/370 1.1.1 and 
it works.  I also complied it without errors  with COBOL II compiler 
level PP 5668-958 IBM VS COBOL II Release 4.0 09/15/92, but did not 
run it but there should be no problems either.

The first thing is IBM disclaimer, then the code. While I used a nested 
program the second program could be separate and LKED statically or 
dynamically called. In a case like this I like the nested way better. 
An identifier in the USING phrase of the CALL . . . BY REFERENCE 
statement can be a file-name, in addition to a data-name. If the 
identifier is the file-name for a queued sequential file, the COBOL 
compiler passes the address of the data control block (DCB) as this 
entry of the parameter list. The identifier cannot be a VSAM 
file-name. 

File-names as CALL operands are allowed by the compiler as an extension. 
Any use of the extension generally depends on the specific internal 
implementation of the compiler. Control block field settings might 
change in future releases. Any changes made to the control block are 
the user's responsibility and not supported by IBM. 

000100 IDENTIFICATION DIVISION. 
000200 PROGRAM-ID. C1. 
000300 DATE-COMPILED. 
000400 ENVIRONMENT DIVISION. 
000500 INPUT-OUTPUT SECTION. 
000600 FILE-CONTROL. 
000700 SELECT OUT-FILE ASSIGN TO S-OUT. 
000800 DATA DIVISION. 
000900 FILE SECTION. 
001000 FD OUT-FILE 
001100 RECORDING MODE F 
001200 BLOCK CONTAINS 0 RECORDS. 
001300 01 OUT-RECORD PIC X(133). 
001400 WORKING-STORAGE SECTION. 
001500 01 HEAD1. 
001600 05 FILLER PIC X VALUE SPACE. 
001700 05 FILLER PIC X(35) 
001800 VALUE 'Test of output'. 
001900 05 FILLER PIC X(97) VALUE SPACES. 
002000 01 DD-NAMES. 
002100 05 FILLER PIC X(8) VALUE 'DDD01'. 
002200 05 FILLER PIC X(8) VALUE 'DDD02'. 
002300 05 FILLER PIC X(8) VALUE 'DDD03'. 
002400 05 FILLER PIC X(8) VALUE 'DDD04'. 
002500 05 FILLER PIC X(8) VALUE 'DDD05'. 
002600 05 FILLER PIC X(8) VALUE 'DDD06'. 
002700 05 FILLER PIC X(8) VALUE 'DDD07'. 
002800 01 DD-TABLE REDEFINES DD-NAMES. 
002900 05 A-DD-NAME PIC X(8) OCCURS 7 TIMES 
003000 INDEXED BY Y. 
003100 PROCEDURE DIVISION. 
003200 0000-MAINLINE. 
003300 PERFORM VARYING Y FROM 1 BY 1 UNTIL Y > 7 
003400 CALL 'FILCHG' USING OUT-FILE A-DD-NAME(Y) 
003500 OPEN OUTPUT OUT-FILE 
003600 MOVE SPACES TO OUT-RECORD 
003700 WRITE OUT-RECORD AFTER ADVANCING PAGE 
003800 WRITE OUT-RECORD FROM HEAD1 AFTER 1 LINES 
003900 CLOSE OUT-FILE 
004000 END-PERFORM. 
004100 GOBACK. 
004200 IDENTIFICATION DIVISION. 
004300 PROGRAM-ID. FILCHG. 
004400 DATE-COMPILED. 
004500 ENVIRONMENT DIVISION. 
004600 INPUT-OUTPUT SECTION. 
004700 FILE-CONTROL. 
004800 DATA DIVISION. 
004900 FILE SECTION. 
005000 WORKING-STORAGE SECTION. 
005100 LINKAGE SECTION. 
005200 01 PASSED-FILE-AREA. 
005300 05 PARM-LENGTH PIC X(40). 
005400 05 DSN PIC X(8). 
005500 01 DD-NAME PIC X(8). 
005600 PROCEDURE DIVISION USING PASSED-FILE-AREA DD-NAME. 
005700 0000-MAINLINE. 
005800 MOVE DD-NAME TO DSN. 
005900 GOBACK. 
006000 END PROGRAM FILCHG. 
006100 END PROGRAM C1.

In article <6s0p5r$4v0$1@holly.prod.itd.earthlink.net>, jimgall@sprintmail.com 
says...
>
>Hello,
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

<a id="t-6ddb58b9d3"></a>
## Thread: Why COBOL is still there ? — 28 this month / 47 total

_Topics: [`COBOL's future, legacy, and obsolescence`](topics.md#future)_

### Why COBOL is still there ?

- **From:** Kalpataru Barman <kalpa@mail-me.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E77EB4.D4CC116@mail-me.com>`

```
After seeing lot of arguments on COBOL , I just thought
of typing something :

* Easy to make a change in a 10 year old COBOL program, 
  rather than a C program, even if both are not
  documented ( or no indentation ) at all. 
* A person with little programming knowledge can make 
  some sense out of a COBOL program. ( This is 
  where COBOL lacks some so called sophistication as 
  a programming language )
* COBOL is slowly giving place to C++, VB..etc...languages
  for the front-end on-line applications, but, this is 
  because people are moving to more GUI based front-ends
  rather than old 3270 ( 80 x 24 ) character based front ends.
  Lesser number of new developments are done today ( say, in 1998 )
  using COBOL/CICS front end.
* BUT, COBOL is still the number one language for batch 
  processing, at least in IBM world, and which is still
  a big world.
* COBOL is going to survive as long IBM OS is there in the 
  market ( MVS, OS390..). And looks like IBM OS will be the
  there for some more time, as they have come up with 
  Mainframe based httpd servers and products like net.data
  for writing macros to access DB2 data for Web display.

Kalpataru Barman
```

#### ↳ Re: Why COBOL is still there ?

- **From:** riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1998-08-29T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3298240.73240.29992@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<35E77EB4.D4CC116@mail-me.com>`

```
In message <<35E77EB4.D4CC116@mail-me.com>> Kalpataru Barman <kalpa@mail-me.com> writes:

> * COBOL is slowly giving place to C++, VB..etc...languages
>   for the front-end on-line applications, but, this is 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>   using COBOL/CICS front end.

In many cases the move to C++ or VB is slow because they
keep tossing out the project and starting again with something
else.  If they haven't already, they will be starting again
with Java soon.

> * BUT, COBOL is still the number one language for batch 
>   processing, at least in IBM world, and which is still
>   a big world.

It is also fairly large in the Unix and PC interactive
business application world.  Fujitsu has moved to this
market because it sees a good potential - and they also
make a C++ and Java.

While C++ now has a standard (give or take a few signoffs)
it is not one that everyone will like.  The language has
'blown out' and is now far too large to be manageable, it
will follow PL/1 and CPL as being too complex for 
sensible use.

> * COBOL is going to survive as long IBM OS is there in the 
>   market ( MVS, OS390..). And looks like IBM OS will be the
>   there for some more time, as they have come up with 
>   Mainframe based httpd servers and products like net.data
>   for writing macros to access DB2 data for Web display.

The AS/400 is also being given new life.  NT 5 is going
to fail, it will never fullfil the hype heaped on it and
this means that business will have to look for an alternate
application server.  Whatever they choose won't have MS's
proprietry languages and this will be good news for Cobol.
```

```
Putting aside my initial "wise guy" response, I believe that this has the
makings of an interesting and useful topic: How many languages should a
professional programmer know and which should they be? Mr. Tees suggests
that six is the minimum; what would be a practical upper limit? And what
are the arguments in favor of a particular language? I hesitate to ask for
arguments against a particular language because of the temptation to
degenerate from a thoughtful critique to mud-slinging; but thoughtful
critiques are valuable, so I will ask for them, too.

I agree that assembler is certainly good to know; further I would say that
one should know at least one mainframe and one microcomputer version.
(Perhaps also one CISC and one RISC). 

And these questions lead on to another set: how much should a professional
programmer know about the hardware organization of computers and how much
about the applications wherein his or her programs are used?

Perhaps this may be put more simply: now that you have been in the
trenches for years, what was it that school *should* have emphasized?


TIA,
rrg
```

```
Ibis redibis numquam peribis wrote in message ...
>Putting aside my initial "wise guy" response, I believe that this has the
>makings of an interesting and useful topic: How many languages should a
>professional programmer know and which should they be

>And these questions lead on to another set: how much should a professional
>programmer know about the hardware organization of computers and how much
>about the applications wherein his or her programs are used?


Each computer language defines an 'abstract machine. Programs should be
written
to that abstract or virtual machine without regard to the underlying 'real'
machine.
So, in that sense, knowing ANYTHING about the real machine is not good.
All too often, we see statements like: "on my system you *just* do blah blah
and it works". This is not good, albeit sometimes necessary.
```

```
>Putting aside my initial "wise guy" response, I believe that this has the
>makings of an interesting and useful topic: How many languages should a
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>critiques are valuable, so I will ask for them, too.


Well, languages come in families, so perhaps that is the way I would
state it.  Two assemblers -- one a byte oriented computer and one
a word oriented computer.  One RISC or microcode assembler as well
would be useful.  A documentation oriented language -- either Cobol
or PL1,  one math oriented --probably Fortran or basic, a couple of
task oriented to get the hang of it (a stats package or emulation language),
a fast and dirty keystroke language (Lisp or Perl), one spreadsheet, one
database, and, of course, one really good text editor macro facility.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: morphed topic: Which Languages?(was:Re: Why COBOL is still there ?)

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ZKlG1.3969$mb1.4449746@news4.mia.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<3298240.73240.29992@kcbbs.gen.nz> <1998083000084000.UAA21208@ladder01.news.aol.com> <u4V7FK709GA.326@upnetnews03> <6sahj5$heg$1@news.igs.net> <Pine.OSF.3.95q.980829213851.27712J-100000@octane.dasb.fhda.edu> <35E94FAF.1821@erols.com> <Pine.OSF.3.95q.980830114241.880C-100000@octane.dasb.fhda.edu>`

```
Ibis redibis numquam peribis wrote in message ...
>
>Putting aside my initial "wise guy" response, I believe that this has the
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>trenches for years, what was it that school *should* have emphasized?

Have you ever wondered why companies are so often little interested in
graduates with no experience?  It is because programming ability, as
absolutely vital as it is, is only part of what a well rounded programmer
should know.  Anybody who has ever trained newbie programmers, no matter
how hot-shot they feel they are, knows that they are almost useless until
they learn a bit about what should be programmed, and why, and even how.
Most good MIS shops have coding standards and libraries of routines that
the new programmer must become familiar with.  All MIS shops have running
systems that the newbie knows nothing about.  Nobody in their right mind
would trust a newbie to start modifying critical applications without
some time to assimilate what is going on in the system.  Even general
knowledge is often lacking in a newbie.  For example, how to handle money
and batch balancing.  While I value my technical programming skills, I
could write code technically as good as anybody could want 25 years ago,
clean, efficient, etc.  I've mastered a pot full of languages during my
career.  But what I have learned about business, the software development
process, and the judgment I have gained with years of experience, I value
even more.

I'm not sure exactly how schools can best address these issues, but I am
convinced they could do a better job than now.  Nothing can substitute
entirely, for experience, but I believe we could be turning out more well
rounded programmers than we often do.  Academic folks are enamoured with
the 'science' of computers, so we have scads of CS degrees offered.  But
how many 'computer scientists' do we need?  For every computer scientist
we need, we need twenty or fifty programmers in other areas.  I majored
in physics and math at university, so I'm not anti-science.  But I have
had many occasions to wish I had taken more accounting and business type
courses.  Others may disagree, but I would like to see more emphasis put
on these areas, and other practical business issues, in the majority of
computer curriculums.
```

```
they can make some text bounce on the screen they think that are doing some
"major coding".    Throw some code in front of them that does something

I have no doubt COBOL will be providing my paycheck for years to come.
```

<a id="t-ece5db71d6"></a>
## Thread: split keys — 4 msgs

### Re: split keys

- **From:** Vadim Maslov <vadik@siber.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E62D0C.38D02B64@siber.com>`
- **References:** `<6rumus$4pm$1@news.igs.net> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-qXUCdE5UDsVx@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <6rva41$glq@dfw-ixnews10.ix.netcom.com> <6rveus$svn$1@news.igs.net> <6s07a0$hcj@sjx-ixn1.ix.netcom.com> <6s146o$imt$1@news.igs.net>`

```
Donald Tees wrote:
> 
> >ALTERNATE RECORD KEY IS record-key-name-1
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> standard (as outlined above), and could not get it to work.  

The really correct way to convert split keys 
is to replace split key with a list of its components
everywhere where its encountered.

All Fujitsu statements that might potentially take
a split key (READ and START) take the list of keys,
not just one key. I think this is a standard extension
by Fujitsu specifically designed to handle "unsplit" split
keys.

Example:

You have C-KEY = KEY-1 KEY-2 in your file definition
         READ ... KEY IS C-KEY

Replace "C-KEY = KEY-1 KEY-2" with "KEY-1 KEY-2" in the file
definition
and replace "C-KEY" with "KEY-1 KEY-2" in the READ
statement.

By the way, mf2fsc can do this work for you automatically.

You can download evaluation copy from
http://www.siber.com/sct/tools/mf2fsc/

Vadim Maslov
```

#### ↳ Re: split keys

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6s77fh$r93@sjx-ixn2.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<6rumus$4pm$1@news.igs.net> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-qXUCdE5UDsVx@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <6rva41$glq@dfw-ixnews10.ix.netcom.com> <6rveus$svn$1@news.igs.net> <6s07a0$hcj@sjx-ixn1.ix.netcom.com> <6s146o$imt$1@news.igs.net> <35E62D0C.38D02B64@siber.com>`

```

Vadim Maslov wrote in message <35E62D0C.38D02B64@siber.com>...
   <snip>
>All Fujitsu statements that might potentially take
>a split key (READ and START) take the list of keys,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

  This approach is neither Standard nor is it portable to other COBOLs.  Nor
is it the approach that will work with the draft of the next COBOL Standard.
This doesn't mean you shouldn't use it today - for Fujitsu - but it
significantly farther away from what will be Standard (in the next Standard)
than the Micro Focus approach.
```

- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00

- **References:** `<6rumus$4pm$1@news.igs.net> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-qXUCdE5UDsVx@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <6rva41$glq@dfw-ixnews10.ix.netcom.com> <6rveus$svn$1@news.igs.net> <6s07a0$hcj@sjx-ixn1.ix.netcom.com> <6s146o$imt$1@news.igs.net> <35E62D0C.38D02B64@siber.com> <6s77fh$r93@sjx-ixn2.ix.netcom.com>`

```
What I meant to say here, is:

This is a non-stnadard extension of the standard
which helps Fujitsu to address the split key problem.

Whether deviating from standard is good or bad,
I don't know. Deviating from the standard certainly 
helped Micro Focus by making their programs 
difficult to migrate to other compilers.

Vadim Maslov
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: split keys

- **From:** Tom Morrison <t.morrison@liant.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35EAABF4.BF241E46@liant.com>`
- **References:** `<6rumus$4pm$1@news.igs.net> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-qXUCdE5UDsVx@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <6rva41$glq@dfw-ixnews10.ix.netcom.com> <6rveus$svn$1@news.igs.net> <6s07a0$hcj@sjx-ixn1.ix.netcom.com> <6s146o$imt$1@news.igs.net> <35E62D0C.38D02B64@siber.com> <6s77fh$r93@sjx-ixn2.ix.netcom.com>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> 
> Vadim Maslov wrote in message <35E62D0C.38D02B64@siber.com>...
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> than the Micro Focus approach.

One possible way of "staying standard" would be to use a (possibly
large) REPLACE statement which would allow one to code standard I/O
statements in the procedure division, thereby isolating the nonstandard
damage to the SELECT statement, which are usually in copybooks.  Beware
of the scope rules on the REPLACE statement, however.

When Fujitsu later supports the standard, or you wish to change compiler
vendors, you should be able to remove the REPLACE statement, edit the
SELECT statement copybooks, and be done.
```

## Thread: COBOL and Perl — 4 msgs

### Re: COBOL and Perl

- **From:** David Coldrick <davidc@selectst.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.perl.misc,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E6A080.FEE38D2@selectst.com>`
- **References:** `<6r7fi0$9a7$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <6rldhj$na5@lotho.delphi.com> <6rr8ge$5a9$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <35E16365.CF280FEC@hankel.mersinet.co.uk>`

```
Charles,

I think you misunderstand what Perl is. I dismissed it as a pile of junk
a couple of years ago after seeing those weird leading characters ($ for
scalar, % for array, indeed!).

However, I picked up a book in Singapore by one of your countrymen, one
Nigel Chapman, that really blew me away (PERL The Programmer's Guide,
John Wiley and Son). It reveals to an experienced programmer how
valuable and how much *fun* it is to accomplish real programming tasks
in Perl. I've programmed in a lot of languages (including Cobol, PL/1,
etc.) and I've never enjoyed a more productive means of expression.

Highly recommend the book, and the language.

Best regards,
David
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL and Perl

- **From:** Charles F Hankel <charles@hankel.mersinet.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.perl.misc,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E9634C.D23702B@hankel.mersinet.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<6r7fi0$9a7$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <6rldhj$na5@lotho.delphi.com> <6rr8ge$5a9$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <35E16365.CF280FEC@hankel.mersinet.co.uk> <35E6A080.FEE38D2@selectst.com>`

```
David Coldrick wrote:
> 
> Charles,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> scalar, % for array, indeed!).  <...snipped book recommendation...>

I'm not dismissing it as a pile of cack.  What I do object to is that
someone feels a need to promote Perl in an attempt to get an advocacy
war going without understanding the environments that a lot of COBOL
people work in.  As someone else said, they considered Selcopy to be a
language.  So, if I have Selcopy available to me and it is suitable for
a particular task, then I am not about to holler for Perl.  I don't see
Perl as another language but rather as another system tool and certainly
would resist the use of an interpretive language in preference to a
compiled one for final production purposes under most circumstances.

It should also be born in mind that the majority of COBOL programmers in
the world do not actually have a lot of say in what system tools they
use.  They may be able to contribute meaningfully to the debate but
that's usually about it.
```

## Thread: OT: (humor) Dr Suess lives on, even the tech world — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: (humor) Dr Suess lives on, even the tech world

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6s707q$lir@dfw-ixnews10.ix.netcom.com>`

```
Dr. Seuss lives on......even in the tech world.

Computer Instructions by Dr. Seuss

    If a packet hits a pocket on a socket on a port,
    and the bus is interrupted as a very last resort,
    and the address of the memory makes your floppy disk abort,
    then the socket packet pocket has an error to report.

    If your cursor finds a menu item followed by a dash,
    and the double-clicking icon puts your window in the trash,
    and your data is corrupted 'cause the index doesn't hash,
    then your situation's hopeless and your system's gonna crash!

    If the label on the cable on the table at your house
    says the network is connected to the button on your mouse,
    but your packets want to tunnel on another protocol,
    that's repeatedly rejected by the printer down the hall,
    and your screen is all distorted by the side effects of gauss,
    so your icons in the window are as wavy as a souse,
    then you may as well reboot and go out with a bang,
    'cause as sure as I'm a poet,
    the sucker's gonna hang!

    When the copy of your floppy's getting sloppy on the disk,
    and the microcode instructions cause unnecessary risk,
    then you have to flash your memory
    and you'll want to RAM your ROM.
    Quickly turn off the computer
    and be sure to tell your Mom.
```

<a id="t-2b854f2850"></a>
## Thread: IBM mainframe COBOL - how to close an output file and reopen it with a different DSN — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: IBM mainframe COBOL - how to close an output file and reopen it with a different DSN

- **From:** "AM" <AM69107@glaxowellcome.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01bdd2bc$990f18f0$c58c3398@us0071826>`
- **References:** `<6s0p5r$4v0$1@holly.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`

```
I just completed a project request that is similar to this. the process was
to format messages that can be sent through email, notifying various
recipients of late orders. Process use, Cobol II,DB2 in OS390. i needed to
have multiple files to set up my messages because different messages can go
to different recipients (more like your institutions). what i did was
format the files and separate each group by job control cards. and then
submit the jobcontrol cards via iebgener on the next job step to submit it
(checking the return code prior to submitting generated jobs, return code =
2, then no jobs were created). the number of jobs spawned will be equal to
the number of files/email messages generated. in each job generated there
is an iebgener to create the files. BUT, i was limited to 80 bytes length
which is enough for an email message/report formatting. then I save each
job created in a GDG for restart/rerun purposes.

hope this helps.

ps. if you find a way to increase the limit on the length, give me a buzz.

Aedehl

Jim Gallagher <jimgall@sprintmail.com> wrote in article
<6s0p5r$4v0$1@holly.prod.itd.earthlink.net>...
| Hello,
| I am new to the IBM mainframe environment (not new to COBOL)  - I am
writing
| batch programs using COBOL II and DB2 in an OS390 environment.  One
program
| must create a file for each of multiple institutions. The files would be
| named BK.PROD.001INST, BK.PROD.002INST etc. Institution number is a 3
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
|
```

```
 Yesterday,
 All those backups seemed a waste of pay.
 Now my database has gone away.
 Oh I believe in yesterday.
 
 Suddenly,
 There's not half the files there used to be,
 And there's a milestone hanging over me
 The system crashed so suddenly.
 
 I pushed something wrong
 What it was I could not say.
 
 Now all my data's gone
 and I long for yesterday-ay-ay-ay.

 The need for back-ups seemed so far away.
 I knew my data was all here to stay,
```

<a id="t-fad7d853b7"></a>
## Thread: COBOL for MVS question — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Re: COBOL for MVS question

- **From:** rkrayhawk@aol.com (RKRayhawk)
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998082804281500.AAA24003@ladder01.news.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<35DB5C13.176E@ibm.net>`

```
The  comments you are receving are exactly correct.  The new COBOL execution
environments in OS/390 must have LE, and the whole shooting match is RES.  

COBOL sevice modules are now less modules and more vectors, a la message
handling in primitive object oriented programming environments.

A call to a service routine is not so much resolvable as an address to goto but
a set of functions guaranteed by the product that must be performed before
control is returned.

In the message oriented program design context you do not construct solidified
executable behemoths.  Instead your deploy large arrays of executable
components that coalesce dynamically into a fluid mosaic.

With reference to the language service modules, the hard link is obsolete.
Right behind that is the fact that erstwhile traditional application managment
styles of statically linked application code is also, I regret to opine, passe.

You overall strategy has technical challenges in the new world.  Your target
market may not have COBOL, but it will likely have LE.

There are ways to get LE in pre-OS/390 environs (LE/370 for MVS ESA, for
example).  But the window will shut upon that course for your clients.

For the intermediate, a possible combination approach may involve back-versions
of the linker/binder for clients not yet on OS/390, to support their LE/370,
and forward- version  linker/binder for those racing ahead into Y2K compliance
early with OS/390.

Fortunately, the COBOL 85 standard syntax COBOL code you develop should be
deployable across this temporary morass, as long as you do not rely on the
FUNCTION jazz.
(Although complete Y2K compliance on behalf of your more modern customers may
require certain alternations in select soure modules).

You can do all of this, but if you are like the rest of us, resources are
short, the new COBOL and LE learning curve is steep and long, ... so really,
... the best thing is to keep it simple.  Target one environment; the full Y2K
compliant environment, OS/390.  OS/390 comes with LE in towe.

It is a fact that the extremely broad based switch to the new technologies can
have a devastating effect on some companies markets. As a technician, you may
be the first to fully recognize the implications, not top management, and not
the product marketers.

It may seem an inconveneience, but your clients need to get to Y2K right
quickly. If they are not talking OS/390, you need to help them.  If they are
not talking OS/390 soon, you  need to help them.

If you are planning to relaease hard linked monoliths some one needs to help
you.  Trust me,  I know that it is not easy to change such a fundamental
concept as your link strategy.
If you respond in time, your product will have a bright future.

By the way, the FCC abend problem mentioned by one of the responders to this
thread is very important. It can bring a Y2K project to a dead halt for an
intolerable period of time.
All hands could potentially be idle while the organization swallows that pill. 


The Abend basically means that some module (a COBOL service module or
LE/370/390 gizmo) has hauled off and tried to execute an SVC that is not in the
hardware.  SSCCRREECCHH!
 
It is a fact that many COBOL application projects on planet earth do not even
have in their possession a single piece of paper with a single word on it that
would give even the slightest clue as to what this is.

It can happen in neat situations like COBOL programs with internal sorts, where
the sort ultimately invokes an access method (which takes us back to COBOL
sevice) routines.

If the victim has SyncSort, they think it is not Y2K compliant or that the
manuals need updating.

When the support gurus finnally recover from this braod-side, the answer that
eminates from their quarters natually leaves the COBOLers asking "what's an
SVC?"













Robert Rayhawk
RKRayhawk@aol.com
```

## Thread: RPG? What is the new fever... — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`AS/400, iSeries, RPG`](topics.md#as400)_

### Re: RPG? What is the new fever...

- **From:** Rick Ritchie <monsters@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E67BDD.8F0B2054@ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<6rndjv$eqs@lotho.delphi.com> <1998082318383200.OAA18281@ladder03.news.aol.com>`

```
The increased function found in the RPG language has been a result of IBM
including new commands that provide the language exisiting COBOL functions.

Examples:   EXSR (execute Subroutine)...........PERFORM
                  DOUEQ (Do Until Equal)..............PERFORM Until
                  DOWEQ (Do While )    ................Another perform
                  READE.. (READ Equal)................Keyed Read
                  READP   (READ Prior).................Well you know
                  Etc Etc Etc.

RPG does not have any object Oriented modules
The lastest version is RPG/400 ILE which is really a rewrite of the language
giving it some "Free Form" capabilities.  They keep trying to turn it into COBOL
with a different name.

Lknout4nm1 wrote:

> Of course there is entire systems built in it.
> I did RPG in the early 90's, and everything from reports to batch to online
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> >
```

## Thread: Basic to COBOL ????? — 5 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Basic to COBOL ?????

- **From:** Rick Ritchie <monsters@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35E69017.AED2D20D@ix.netcom.com>`

```
I am not familiar with the BASIC language but need to reproduce a basic
program in COBOL (MF 3.0) any suggestions? The program accesses a board
in the PC that sends a signal to a switch to put it in the open or close
position. The board does this by sending a current through the line to
hold the switch open until the "off" mode is tripped. Here is the code
for the BASIC program that I need to duplicate.

CLS
b = &H300
OUT  b + 3, 128 ' turn all to output

start:
PRINT "ON"
OUT b + 1, 128 ' 16=7, pin 6=8,k2, pin 3=128, k1-pb7, pin 4=64, k5
i$ = INPUT$(1)

PRINT "OFF"
OUT = b + 1,0
i$ = INPUT$
GOTO start

I have tried the X"88" function but I'm not sure I have the correct port
address or how to direct the action of certain pins. If I do a run in
basic the decial value displayed from the &H300 is 768.

Thanks for your help.
```

```

Rick Ritchie wrote in message <35E69017.AED2D20D@ix.netcom.com>...
```

#### ↳ Re: Basic to COBOL ?????

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@aiservices.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<QcyF1.738$Kt4.4322740@news1.atlantic.net>`
- **References:** `<35E69017.AED2D20D@ix.netcom.com>`

```

Rick Ritchie wrote in message <35E69017.AED2D20D@ix.netcom.com>...
>I am not familiar with the BASIC language but need to reproduce a basic
>program in COBOL (MF 3.0) any suggestions? The program accesses a board
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>OUT b + 1, 128 ' 16=7, pin 6=8,k2, pin 3=128, k1-pb7, pin 4=64, k5

This comment appears to give the values for relays connected to certain
pins. I would guess that "pin 6" has a value of "8" with a designation of
"k2"
and so on. Though the "16=7" does not help!

>i$ = INPUT$(1)
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Hope this helps.
--------------
       program-id. port300.
       data division.
       working-storage section.
       01 port-addresses.
         03 port-301 pic 9(5) value 301. *> b+1
         03 port-303 pic 9(5) value 303. *> b+3
       01 output-values.
         03 relay-1-on pic x value x"80".  *> 128
         03 relay-2-on pic x value x"08".  *> 8
         03 relay-5-on pic x value x"40".  *> 64
         03 relay-off pic x value x"00". *> 0
       01 keyboard-input pic x.
       procedure division.
           display space upon crt. *> CLS
           call x"88" using port-303 relay-on. *> OUT

       start-of-loop.
           display "ON". *> PRINT
           call x"88" using port-301 relay-1-on. *> OUT
           accept keyboard-input. *> INPUT$ not the same as INPUT$(1)

           display "OFF". *> PRINT
           call x"88" using port-301 relay-off. *> OUT
           accept keyboard-input. *> INPUT$

           go to start-of-loop. *> GOTO
           stop run.
-------------------------------
Rick Smith
e-mail: < ricksmith@aiservices.com >
```

## Thread: Seeking recommendations: COBOL — 3 msgs

### Seeking recommendations: COBOL

- **From:** schatham@interpath.com (Steve)
- **Date:** 1998-08-29T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35e7e6e2.85741836@news.interpath.net>`

```


I'm wanting to do some coding on my PC, and am seeking a COBOL
compiler that will let me develop code on the PC for DOS, Windows 3.1+
and 95/NT.  

However, the main requirements that I have are:
I would like it to be portable (i.e. distribute the EXEcutable files),
and have reasonably good interfacing with other products (other COBOL
compilers), and mainly, that I don't have to break the bank to buy it.

Can anyone make some suggestions?

Any help would be appreciated.

Thanks,

Steve

schatham@interpath.com
```

#### ↳ Re: Seeking recommendations: COBOL

- **From:** redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-KwkJLE8hj1zb@Dwight_Miller.iix.com>`
- **References:** `<35e7e6e2.85741836@news.interpath.net>`

```
On Sat, 29 Aug 1998 11:32:56, schatham@interpath.com (Steve) wrote:

> 
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Obviously people are not reading your requirements.   A Windows only 
compiler such as MF Personal COBOL for Windows, and Fujitsu do not 
meet your needs.

You are wanting to mix apples and oranges.  If you want NT support, 
you probably desire 32 bit COBOL - which means it WON'T make DOS 
programs.

However, if you want DOS programs you need a 16 bit compiler.  The 16 
bit offerings from MicroFocus and CA-Realia fit this bill.  You can 
create 16 bit Windows executables that will work under NT with each of
these.

The one with the widest platform support is AccuCOBOL, but it is also 
the one with the largest per install cost.

If you can back off the the DOS requirement, there are more choices 
available at a more reasonable price.
```

#### ↳ Re: Seeking recommendations: COBOL

- **From:** ckpbb@aol.com (Ckpbb)
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998083100064400.UAA10840@ladder01.news.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<35e7e6e2.85741836@news.interpath.net>`

```
Check out Personal COBOL for Windows.  You can get this from MicroFocus.  I
don't know much about it because I'm just getting into it, myself, but I do
know the cost is affordable.  You may be able to get an Educational Copy, which
is more affordable.  You can program Structured and OO COBOL.
```

<a id="t-a666b19a52"></a>
## Thread: alpha-num to signed numeric — 3 msgs

### Re: alpha-num to signed numeric

- **From:** sff5ky@aol.com (Sff5ky)
- **Date:** 1998-08-29T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998082920061000.QAA22471@ladder01.news.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<35eb52f5.14009432@news.mindspring.com>`

```
In article <35eb52f5.14009432@news.mindspring.com>, laelia@mindspring.com
writes:

>I'm trying to easily move an alphanumeric number (it's reading in a
>133 char report file) in which some of the report numbers have a
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>

   Do you want this '-1bbbbb' to be stored as a negative 1 or negative 100000?
   It appears that your program is performing as directed. Replacing spaces
with
   zeroes changes the number value such that your initial -1 becomes -100000
   prior to being moved to the destination field.
   I would suggest trying FUNCTION NUMVAL or writing a right justification 
   routine to shift numeric characters and then multiply the result by -1 if a 
   negative sign was found.
   You could also try defining the input field as a numeric edited '--------9'
   and then use a straight move to your zone-decimal numeric field.  I have 
   not had problems doing this although a packed-decimal receiver might create 
   problems.
```

#### ↳ Re: alpha-num to signed numeric

- **From:** riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3298241.69432.15615@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<1998082920061000.QAA22471@ladder01.news.aol.com>`

```
In message <<1998082920061000.QAA22471@ladder01.news.aol.com>> sff5ky@aol.com  writes:

>    You could also try defining the input field as a numeric edited '--------9'
>    and then use a straight move to your zone-decimal numeric field.  I have 
>    not had problems doing this although a packed-decimal receiver might create 
>    problems.

I understand that de-editing will become part of the next
standard, but currently it is an extension that, with MF,
requires a compiler option DE-EDIT"1".  It is valid to use
aligned numeric edited moves in ANSI85, but not misaligned.
```

## Thread: Incorrect data-name in REDEFINES clause — 3 this month / 8 total

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### Re: Incorrect data-name in REDEFINES clause

- **From:** jraben19@mail.idt.net (Jeff Raben)
- **Date:** 1998-08-29T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35e85cf3.1544545@news.idt.net>`
- **References:** `<35df03f8.20027739@netnews.worldnet.att.net>`

```
Vern_Bradner@newcourt.com (Vern Bradner) wrote:

>Hi,
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>
1) 01 levels are never redefined under an FD/SD

2) WORKING-STORAGE has nothing to do with a file / record except at a
READ.... INTO...
They are in 2 different "address spaces" in particular in most COBOL's
the 01 record area is acquired dynamically.

JR
and stir with a Runcible spoon...
```

#### ↳ Re: Incorrect data-name in REDEFINES cla

- **From:** riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3298241.69907.15830@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<35e85cf3.1544545@news.idt.net>`

```
In message <<35e85cf3.1544545@news.idt.net>> jraben19@mail.idt.net  writes:
> >
> 1) 01 levels are never redefined under an FD/SD

They can be, they may be, they don't need to be.

> 
> 2) WORKING-STORAGE has nothing to do with a file / record except at a
> READ.... INTO...
> They are in 2 different "address spaces" in particular in most COBOL's
> the 01 record area is acquired dynamically.

Regardless of whether the record area is statically created
or dynamically created it must always behave in a valid
way.  The implication of being 'acquired dynamically' is
that sometimes (such as prior to OPEN or after CLOSE or
END) it does not exist.  I do not believe that a record
area that is non-existant during part of a program run
would match the expected behaviour of standard Cobol.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Incorrect data-name in REDEFINES cla

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6scnt7$lfj@dfw-ixnews9.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<35e85cf3.1544545@news.idt.net> <3298241.69907.15830@kcbbs.gen.nz>`

```

Richard Plinston wrote in message <3298241.69907.15830@kcbbs.gen.nz>...
>In message <<35e85cf3.1544545@news.idt.net>> jraben19@mail.idt.net  writes:
>> >
>> 1) 01 levels are never redefined under an FD/SD
>
>They can be, they may be, they don't need to be.
   No, not in a Standard COBOL program.  A REDEFINES at the 01-level is
ILLEGAL in Standard COBOL (but allowed as an extension in others - this was
actually a major migration headache for IBM environment programmers going
from OS/VS COBOL to VS COBOL II - the former allowed in and the latter does
not)

>
>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Guess again.
   The Standard is quite clear that not only are the contents of the Record
area undefined before an OPEN or after a CLOSE - but even attempting to
access them is "unpredictable".  Many current compilers will cause run-time
termination (usually abnormal) if you try this.
```

```
    05  NEXT-FIELD		PIC X(10)
```

```
I'll use it when it's free! I work for a bank, so my checking account is
completely free (I pay a service charge if I bounce a check, of course).
Online banking costs substantially more, even with an employee discount. 

BTW, I have heard that much of what's touted as "online banking" is
actually done by paper and you have to make your funds available up
front. So the paying service has your funds (and whatever interest they
might earn waiting to clear) and you have no guarantee that your payees
will be paid on time.

What are the pluses? Seriously, what do you like about it?

Bill Lynch
```

```
Bill Lynch wrote:
> 
> Paul Knudsen wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> will be paid on time.

Yep.  In fact, the billpay company makes more money if they send paper checks
then if they pay electronicly, because they get the "float" from the time they
get the money from the customer and the time the check actually clears.  It's
very wierd.  We're (I work for a bank also) working on setting up this type
of thing with Checkfree as the bill pay company.  They claim that they attempt
to sign up big companies for doing ACH payments, but I'm guessing that the 
companies would have to pay *them* to do it.
 
> What are the pluses? Seriously, what do you like about it?

I personally like ACH payments when the company debits my account.  It saves me
the hassle of writing the checks, and usually they don't debit until the actual
due date.  With online billpay it seems to me you still have to make some effort,
because the amounts change each month.  Dunno if it's worth it.  When we get it
up and running they want the programmers and other employees to help test it out
in "pilot" mode, so I'll let you know.  (Obviously it will be free at that point.
Not sure if we'll be charged after that.)
```

```
Bill Lynch wrote:
>Paul Knudsen wrote:
>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>What are the pluses? Seriously, what do you like about it?


My bank does take the money out of your account the day the payment is
issued, but they guarantee that the payment will arrive on time, as long
as you give them 5 business days to process it.  I recently bought a lot
of computer equipment in one month and had a $10,000 credit card bill.
I made the payment in time, but the credit card company had changed their
P.O. box number, so after the payment was forwarded it was past due.  The
bank paid the $100 interest.

But I like it for other reasons, too.  I have an equity account that I
use for capital expenditures and cash flow management, and I can make
advances and payments on that account online, and transfer money around
my other accounts too.  I have a team of programmers working for me on
Y2K remediation, and I pay them with transfers.  They all have accounts
at the same bank as me, so the money goes in the same day, just like
direct deposit. :-)  My checking account draws interest, so when I get
a bill, I enter the payment to be processed exactly five business days
before the due date, then forget about it.  If it is a payment to my
bank's Visa, I can date it on the due date.  I can check on my balances
any time day or night, and schedule payments months in advance if I want.
I can see transactions back for weeks on any account.  It is free to me,
because I just turned 50 ($8/mo. otherwise).  I pay my parent's bills for
them, and I just set up another online banking account, using the same
program.  Right now, it does not go through the Internet, but it's a
direct call to a bank number.  I like the security of that.  It is an
800 number, so I can call it free from anywhere in the U.S.
```

```
OK - the off-topic police will be after me, but here goes.....

Depends entirely on the bank and the service.  I use Quicken with
CoreStates (now First Union) as my bank.  There are a few bills that
are paid electronically, but usually a check on my account is cut and
mailed.  Funds come out of my account when the check clears.  I get
the canceled checks back with my statement.  Cost is $3.95 per month
for unlimited online banking and bill paying.  I've only had two
payments arrive late or not at all in the 3 (4?) years I've been using
them.  When I got the late notices, I simply e-mailed Customer Service
at CoreStates, and they took care of it.  No late fees, no stop
payment fees, no blotch on my record with the payee (I checked).

What I really like about it (aside from no real problems so far) is
that as soon as I receive a bill in the mail, I enter the payment,
give the date to be paid by, transmit it and then forget about it.
Takes about a minute or two, and I don't have to find the time to sit
down, write the checks, find/buy stamps, and then remember to mail the
checks on time.  Very convenient if you're traveling a lot.

Now, before CoreStates, I tried online banking and bill-paying with
NationsBank.  Unless they've improved their process greatly, I'd be
hesitant to recommend them since they operate more like what you were
describing.

Hope this helps.
```

#### ↳ Re: ok, heres one...

- **From:** gchomuk@my-dejanews.com
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6sf631$e9b$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`
- **References:** `<35EA0537.9A5E33D5@mindspring.com>`

```
In article <35EA0537.9A5E33D5@mindspring.com>,
  Mike Ganas <skidmike@mindspring.com> wrote:
> back to the muffler shop system.  we want to keep estimates on file in
> hopes that the customer comes back.  but the estimates need to be
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Sounds suspiciously like MS-Cobol.  There must be a way to issue a DOS delete
of the file. C, C++ has the SYSTEM("DELETE"), or SHELLEXT("DELETE") function.

MS-COBOL should have something similar.  Opening the Closing the file, as you
indicated, leaves a directory entry of zero length.  You need to issue a
system call to issue the delete.  You'll have to close the file, of course,
prior to deleting.

-----== Posted via Deja News, The Leader in Internet Discussion ==-----
http://www.dejanews.com/rg_mkgrp.xp   Create Your Own Free Member Forum
```

#### ↳ Re: ok, heres one...

- **From:** "COBOL Frog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35EB1257.225DA5E2@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<35EA0537.9A5E33D5@mindspring.com>`

```
Mike Ganas wrote:

> back to the muffler shop system.  we want to keep estimates on file in
> hopes that the customer comes back.  but the estimates need to be
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> well?

COBOL does not have external file handling capacities, like delete, rename,
move, copy etc. the only way to do is  calling some system utility or a
program written in another language that has those capabilities.

But it is platform dependent how and what to call.
On IBM mainframe, invoke some JCL, f.e. the IEFBR14 that The Goobers
mentioned in his reply post.
On PC's with DOS/WinX you could call (via command.com f.e.) some batch file.
From Micro Focus I know that they have some special routines provided, like
CBL_CHECK_FILE_EXIT, CBL_DELETE_FILE, CBL_RENAME_FILE (and for DIR's as
well). Probably fujitsu, acuCOBOL and others have some stuff too...

Does this help?

You could also redesign to one big file containing all customer's records,
preferably ordered by customer/date and precess that with OPEN I-O and the
DELETE record statements.

The COBOL Frog
```

```
Donald Tees schrieb:

> With any Cobol I have ever used, this is not true. After an open
> input, a read next automatically starts at the beginning.  The start
> only need be used when one has already read a record.

on BTRIEVE-Files (DOS, WINx, OS/2) the open-command
do *NOT* set any record-pointer. So you have to set it
with the START command.

You have to set the key also if it is not the primary-key.
```

### Help with compute

```
The following Cobol program gives different results on different compilers.
What should it give?
On Realia and MicroFocus the two displays are the same.
On Fujitsu, the second on shows zeroes.
What does the standard say here?
Or are intermediate results 'implementor defined'?

000100 IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
000200 PROGRAM-ID.    TT.
000300
000400 AUTHOR.        LEIF SVALGAARD.
000500
000600 DATE-WRITTEN.  98/08/30
000700     -REVISED:  98/08/30.

000900 ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.

001100 CONFIGURATION SECTION.

001400

001800

002000     02  THE-SIZE                PIC S9(7)  COMP  VALUE +5000000.
002100     02  THE-RESULT              PIC S9(7)  COMP.
002300     02  RANDOM-INTEGER          PIC S9(7)  COMP  VALUE +1234567.
002400     02  RANDOM-MODULUS          PIC S9(7)  COMP  VALUE +2099863.

003400                        / RANDOM-MODULUS

003700                        * THE-SIZE
```

#### ↳ Re: Help with compute

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6scbp4$3cn$1@news01.btx.dtag.de>`
- **References:** `<35e9a5b4.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

```

#### ↳ Re: Help with compute

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<JFiG1.3809$_B3.4376878@news2.mia.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<35e9a5b4.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```
Welcome to the wonderful world of expression evaluation! ;-)

What is likely happening is that, because all arguments are integers,
Fujitsu COBOL is storing the intermediate results in an integer.
Since RANDOM-INTEGER < RANDOM-MODULUS, the intermediate result of the
divide is zero which, when multiplied by THE-SIZE returns zero.

Because of this, it is usually advisable to place your multiplies
before your divides (or nest them in parenthesis to force earlier
evaluation), so long as you do not generate an intermediate result
which is *too* large and is truncated.  I don't have Fujitsu COBOL
to check, but there is a good chance that if you define one of the
data names (e.g. RANDOM-INTEGER) with a V9 on the end, it will work
correctly.  This alerts the compiler to provide for non integer
intermediate results.  Believe it or not, figuring out just how to
evaluate a particular expression without truncation or overflow is
not a trivial problem for the compiler to solve.  It's much easier
with real variables, of course.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Help with compute

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Help with compute

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@aiservices.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<U6kG1.1132$Kt4.5466240@news1.atlantic.net>`
- **References:** `<35e9a5b4.0@news1.ibm.net> <JFiG1.3809$_B3.4376878@news2.mia.bellsouth.net> <35e9bd28.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```

Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <35e9bd28.0@news1.ibm.net>...
>Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>>Welcome to the wonderful world of expression evaluation! ;-)
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>considered?

Committee Draft 1.2 provides for "native" arithmetic and "standard"
arithmetic. Native is defined by the implementor. Standard specifies
that intermediate results shall have at least 32 significant digits and
an exponent with a range of plus or minus 100.

If you want to get a better idea how each of your current compilers
processes intermediate results, look for the section on "arithmetic
expressions." I find it interesting that MicroFocus uses two methods;
one of which is selected based upon compatibilty with other systems.
-------------------------------
Rick Smith
e-mail: < ricksmith@aiservices.com >
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Help with compute

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6scnkq$l9a@dfw-ixnews9.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<35e9a5b4.0@news1.ibm.net> <JFiG1.3809$_B3.4376878@news2.mia.bellsouth.net> <35e9bd28.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```

Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <35e9bd28.0@news1.ibm.net>...
>Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>>Welcome to the wonderful world of expression evaluation! ;-)
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>


   and the good news is that the draft Standard has a new option called
"Arithmetic is Standard" which (I think) will impact or at least give a
portable result for this statement.  A few things to note:

  1) There are a LOT of rules for Standard Arithmetic - so I am not positive
that it applies in this sample.  (You can look at the draft if you want to
figure it out for yourself)

   2)  There is some concern that the overhead for Standard Arithmetic will
be so great that in the "real world" no one will ever use it.  (It requires
that most arithmetic be done in a floating point format with 32 digits of
precision.  This is a very imprecise description of it, so please don't get
excited from this wording.)

 3) For programs coded today, the default will remain "ARITHMETIC IS NATIVE"
which means you will get the same un-portable results as today.

P.S.  If you want me to forward your original sample to the person who
understands Standard Arithmetic the best - and ask him what the results will
be, I will try and do so.
```

```
at all), and that came with a 500-page book--but not the COBOL.  .It
is a CD, which came with a small booklet.  The compiler is
ACUCOBOL-85.  As far as I can remember my COBOL, from the list of
reserved words, it looks like a pretty good compiler, but I have no
idea how to start using it!!!

Allen J. Bennett
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to start

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-08-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6sc7ij$r20$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<35e8d398.165774907@news.primenet.com> <6sbl00$rkq$1@news.igs.net> <35e966b9.203436618@news.primenet.com>`

```
>is a CD, which came with a small booklet.  The compiler is
>ACUCOBOL-85.  As far as I can remember my COBOL, from the list of
>reserved words, it looks like a pretty good compiler, but I have no
>idea how to start using it!!!
>
Do you have any experience with compilers at all, or you a complete
neophyte?  There are usually some examples on a CD of that sort.
The norm is to find a program called "setup" or "install", then start
working your way through the examples.

If you have only used a language like Basic, then the first thing you are
going to have to get the hang of is the structure.  Cobol is a language
of it's own.  You write a program using a text editor, then compile
and link it ... do you understand those concepts?
```

<a id="t-a8b24d1fa7"></a>
## Thread: working in the buffers — 1 this month / 2 total

### Re: working in the buffers

- **From:** redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)
- **Date:** 1998-08-31T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-1hvpjDj3F6uV@Dwight_Miller.iix.com>`
- **References:** `<35df03f8.20027739@netnews.worldnet.att.net> <6rn21n$cpt$1@nnrp03.primenet.com> <35E1F0DE.714C@hpohp2.wgw.bt.co.uk>`

```
On Mon, 24 Aug 1998 23:01:50, Jeremy Coast 
<coastjf@hpohp2.wgw.bt.co.uk> wrote:

>> 
> Mike,
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Regards,


I'm not Mike - but having been recently bitten, I'll take a stab at 
it.

First there are a few places where you get into trouble on some 
implenentations.  You can't move data to the buffer before a file is 
opened or after it is closed.

I have a habit of not opening a file in a particular program until its
first use.  Very clean and efficient, but don't try to build the 
record in the buffer before the file is open!

Secondly, wrote a program, simple file in and out, and - read a 
record, moved some fields and created others, then wrote the record.  
Simple.

I then went back into this program and added, without thinking, some 
post write processing.

The records I referenced in my processing were to be the ones just 
written. This is a serious No No.  The actual data my program found 
was from several hundred records ago.  It was a real mess.  Just 
produced strange, very strange results.

If I had not worked in the buffers - as a rule - I would not have seen
this problem.

However, do I still advocate buffer abstinance ?  No.  I think if you 
follow the rules, and understand what is happening, you can safely 
work in the buffers.  Just don't address the area when the file is 
closed, and don't reference the data in the buffer after a write.

Blast away.
```

---

```
nick@asheath.demon.co.uk (Nick & Carol Lucas-White) wrote:
```
